DAN GL1AD-E Sil
APPRAISAL OF ASIHUG'ANJ FERTILIZER P'ROJECT
STIJ4ARY XND CONCLUSIONS
i.        This report appraises thle proposed construiction of an ammonia/urea
fertilizer plant in Bangladesh.  The plant would have a 925 metric tons per
day (TPD) ammonia un.it and a 1,600 TPD urea unit and use natural gas fron a
nearby field as fuel and feedstock.  It would be built at Ashuganj, about
60 km northeast of Dacca. A new company, the Ashuganj Fertilizer and
Chemical Company (AFCC) was recently established to own and operate the plant.
All shares will be held by the Government of Bangladesh. The proposed project
would be the Bank Group's largest direct investment in the country's industry.
ii.       The objectives of the project are to help meet the country's need
for nitrogen fertilizer at an economic cost by using Bangladesh's abundant
and highly suitable reserves of natural gas and to eliminate its dependence
on costly nitrogen fertilizer imports. Given the limited acreage of arable
land and the growing population, the increased use of fertilizer is of criti-
cal importance for the country to feed its people and to increase the output
of cash crops, which is the mainstay of exports at present and in the fore-
seeable future.
iii.     IUntil the new company is sufficiently staffed, the Bangladesh Fer-
tilizer, Chemical and Pharmaceutical Corporation (BFCPC) will be responsible
for project execution. BFCPC presently controls and supervises two urea plants,
at Ghorasal and Fenchuganj and two triple superphosphate (TSP) plants at
Chittagong. In addition 48 chemical and pharmaceutical enterprises, either
left by their former Pakistani owners, or inherited from the East Pakistan
Industrial Development Corporation (EPIDC) were put under BFCPC's umbrella
after independence in 1972. The two existing urea plants need some recon-
ditioning and the Government is taking steps to rehabilitate these units with
external assistance. But the larger of these plants is not expected to resume
production until mid-1975 due to an explosion in its control room. Arrange-
ments have been made to replace the damaged facilities expeditiously. One TSP
plant only recently started trial production while the second unit will under-
go trial production in February 1975 and IDA is helping finance the import
of phosphate rock and sulfur to continue TSP production; thereafter these
plants are expected to have a combined output of 352,000 tons per year (TPY)
of urea and 135,000 TPY of TSP, to which the proposed project would add
anotlher 475,000 TPY of urea.
iv.       The use of fertilizer has rapidly grown in Bangladesh.  Between
1963/64 and 1970/71 total nutrient use increased from 47,900 to 143,000 TPY.
It dropped drastically during the war of independence due to disruptions of
iimports and production but rose sharply to 176,400 nutrient tons in 1972/73,
in spite of the aftermath of war, a severe drought and the general poverty
of the farmers.  Urea consumption in that year came to 272,000 tons, equiva-
lent to 125,000 tons of N, or 71% of total ritutrient use, and shortfalls in
output of the existing plants had to be made up by imnports, for which only



limited funds were available. Furthermore, consumption may well understate
demand since despite sharply increasing fertilizer prices a black market in
urea has developed.
V.        Projecting a conservative demand growth rate of 12.3% over the next
ten years compared with one of 1.3.6% observed during the past decade, supply
and demand would be about in balance in 1981/82; this would leave temporarily
an exportable surplus of up to 100,000 tons per year from 1979/80 to 1980/81,
depending on the output achieved by the existing plants after reconditioning
and the speed with which the proposed project can be brought to full output.
The market, however, is not cons:.dered a constraint, since the project would
be able to sell at competitive prices and any exportable surplus should find
a ready market in India or elswhere in the region.  Marketing and distribution
of the project's output down to the Thana (county) level is the responsi-
bility of the Bangladesh Agricultural Development Corporation (BADC). The
Government has initiated a comprEhensive fertilizer marketing and distribution
study to identify the additional steps needed to assure efficient marketing
and distribution of indigenous ard imported fertilizer. The first phase of
the study, financed by USAID, was started April 1974 by the Tennessee Valley
Authority (TVA) and its report is expected early 1975.
vi.       The project is estimated to cost about US$249 million equivalent,
of which some US$142 million would be in foreign exchange. The Association
assisted the Government in arranging the necessary foreign loans to be pro-
vided by the Asian Development Bank (ADB - US$30 million); the United States
Agency for International Development (AID - US$30 million); the United King-
dom Ministry of Overseas Development (ODM - US$18 million); the Government
of Iran (GOI - US$12.4 million); the Government of the Federal Republic of
Germany acting through Kreditanstalt fuer Wiederaufbau; (KfW-US$12 million);
the Government of Switzerland (GOi - US$6.5 million) and the Association (US$33
million). Local currency costs estimated at US$107 million would be covered
by the Government from its own re~sources as equity. All foreign loans would
be made to the Government at concessional terms and would be relent to the
Company which would carry the foreign exchange risk at 10% interest for a
term of 15 years, including 5 yea::s grace.
vii.      The project was first conceived by EPIDC as part of a larger petro-
cliemical complex and assumed its present scope in a dialogue over the past
two years between the Government, BFCPC and the Association. The plant would
employ modern, fully-proven technology and take, at the proposed capacities,
full advantages of economies of scale. BFCPC after examining six alternative
sites has chosen the proposed locc.tion on the eastern bank of the Meghna
River near Ashuganj which, while requiring considerable fill, has the ad-
vantage of being centrally locatec with respect to marketing areas, with
access to almost all parts of the country by rail and riverways, and permit-
ting seacoasters to load at its jetty. Additionally, the Titas gasfield,
which is to supply the plant, and has ample reserves, is only about 12 km
away and a transmission line withi sufficient spare capacity passes the pro-
posed plant site nearby. The Ashuganj Power Plant with sufficient spare
capacity is located about 3 km from the proposed site and the Meghna River
would provide the water needed for cooling, processing and drinking purposes.



- ili -
viii.     BFCPC intends to appoint an Engineering Firm who would have full
responsibility for implementing the project, including the provision of
process 'Licenses, basic and detailed engineering and the management of pro-
curement, construction, start-up, commissioning and training of Bangladesh
staff, and who would provide assistance in initial operation. Invitations
to bid, approved by all lenders, were issued by BFCPC to prequalified en-
gineering firms and proposals are due by February 1975. To strengthen its
technical capability, BFCPC has engaged a consulting firm (a joint venture
between Scientific Design Co., Ltd., U.K. and James Chemical Engineering,
US) to act as its Technical Advisor. It is estimated that the plant will
begin commercial operations in August 1978.
ix.       The project's capitalization based on a stipulated debt/equity
ratio of 60/40 is sound. An ex-plant price of US$155 per ton of bagged urea
is expected to meet a debt service coverage ratio of at least 1.5, and yield
a financial rate of return of 9.2% (13.8% before income taxes). The com-
parativelv low rate reflects the high cost of a new plant requiring extensive
site development; the absence of any relief from import duty (approximately
40,) or tax incentives often found in other developing countries; low ex-
factory price; and a high rate of corporate tax (55%). The Company however,
is expected to be in a comfortable financial position after the first operat-
Ing year, accumulate sizable cash reserves, pay dividends from the second
year of operation onwards and/or build up funds for further expansion. The
Government sets the fertilizer prices, wage rates, power charges and the
natural gas price, and the project's financial viability will be safeguarded
through a combination of a maximum debt/equity ratio, a reasonable return on
equity and minimum debt service coverage and current ratios. The financial
rate of return is not very sensitive to moderately adverse developments
involving most assumptions in the financial analysis. It would change by
one percentage point with each increase of US$15 per ton in the ex-plant
price of urea and drop to 6.7% (after taxes) with a one year delay in
construction and a total project cost increase of 20%.
x.        The economic rate of return is 21%Z based on an economic price of
US$175 per ton of urea. nTe return changes by about two percentage points
for each uS$20 difference in the economic price of urea. A 20% cost increase
combined with a one year delay in construction reduces the rate of return
to about 16%. The gross annual foreign exchange savings that can be ascribed
to the project would be about US$74 million or about US$155 per ton of urea.
The project represents very efficient import substitution; approximately
US$50 of local resources are needed to replace US$100 worth of imports.
xi.       There are major risks involved in the project.  To build a
complex fertilizer plant in a country of Bangladesh's state of economic
development is in itself a formidable undertaking. The main risks lie in
further equipment price escalation in a strong sellers' market, delays in
equipment delivery from fully booked suppliers; and most importantly, weak-
nesses in public administration and project management that could result
in costly delays in project implementation and in the time span reasonable
capacity utilization is reached. No country, but least one of Bangladesh's



- iv -
general state of poverty and limited resources, can afford inefficient proj-
ect implementation and the introduction of expatriate expertise in project
management as well as production functions in addition to the Engineering
Firm and the Technical Advisor and their clearly delineated responsibilities
as well as their smooth cooperation with the Bangladesh staff is a sine qua
non for a successful project.
xii.      Agreement was obtained otl such management assistance in a form
satisfactory to the lenders and on the other matters summarized at the end
of this report, and the project is suitable for an IDA credit of US$33
million equivalent.



I. INTRODUCTION
1 .01     The Government of Bangladesh has requested an IDA Credit of US$33
million equivalent for an ammonia-urea fertilizer project expected to cost
US$249 million, including US$142 million in foreign exchange. The balance
of the foreign exchange requirements (US$109 million) would be provided by
the Asian Development Bank (ADB), the United States Agency for International
Development (USAID), the United Kingdom Overseas Development Ministry (ODM),
the Government of Iran (GOI), the Federal Republic of Germany (FRG) through
the Kreditanstalt fuer Wiederaufbau (KfW) and the Government of Switzerland
(GOS). The Government of Bangladesh (GOB) would provide the local currency
cost (US$107 million) from its own resources in the form of equity. All
foreign exchange lending would be extended to the Government on concessional
terms.
1.02      The proceeds from foreign borrowing would be onlent by the Govern-
ment to the Ashuganj Fertilizer and Chemical Company (AFCC), a new company
recently established to build and operate the fertilizer plant. The project,
with a rated capacity of 528,000 tons per year (TPY) of urea, or about 243,000
TPY of nitrogen (N) would increase the country's urea manufacturing capacity
to about 970,000 TPY. The plant would be built at Ashuganj, on the eastern
bank of the Meghna River, in the province of Comilla, about 60 km northeast of
Dacca, with rail and water transport connections to all important agricultural
areas of the country (Map, IBRD 10809). It would use natural gas from the
nearby Titas field as feedstock and fuel. Annex 1 gives a glossary of tech-
nical terms commonly used in the fertilizer industry.
1.03      IDA involvement in the project dates back to early 1970, when the
then East Pakistan Industrial Development Corporation (EPIDC), presented to
the Association a project consisting of a 320,000 TPY urea plant at the same
location as part of a large petrochemical complex to be based on the same
natural gas resources. After the war of independence, the Government of
Bangladesh requested IDA to review a revised project prepared by the newly
formed Bangladesh Fertilizer, Chemical and Pharmaceutical Corporation (BFCPC)
which had taken over EPIDC's role in the fertilizer and other chemical indus-
tries. During project preparation missions in February, July and October
1973 the present project was developed, and in September 1973 the Government
sought the Association's assistance in securing the necessary foreign ex-
change financing.
1.04      The appraisal was carried out in Bangladesh in March/April 1974
by Messrs. Kaupisch (Chief), Evans and Carpio of the Industrial Projects
Department and Harrison and Hpu of the South Asia Region, concurrently with
missions representing ADB, AID and ODM.
II. THE COMPANY
A.   Background, The Bangladesh Fertilizer Industry
2.01      Chemical fertilizer has been used in Bangladesh since 1955 and
demand has been growing at about 15% annually over the past ten years even
after the disruption caused by the 1971 war of independence. To meet these



-2-
requirements, EPIDC completed in 1961 a 106,000 TPY urea plant at Fenchuganj,
and in 1970 a 340,000 TPY urea plant at Ghorasal based on the latest centrif-
ugal compressor technology. Bothl plants use the country's ample reserves of
non-associated, low sulfur natural gas. While the Fenchugani plant operated
satisfactorily for the first nine years, restart after the war showed dis-
appointing results and a major orerhaul is indicated. The Ghorasal plant,
after passing performance tests, encountered mechanical problems and has
only recently achieved 80% of itt; rated capacity; however, it is sz-ill plagued
with frequent breakdowns and recently the factory has been shut down by an
explosion in the control room and is not expected to resume product.ion until
mid-1975. To meet growing phosplhate fertilizer requirements EPIDC completed
in 1969 and 1970 two triple superphosphate (TSP) plants of 32,000 and 120,000
TPY capacity respectively at Chittagong, based on imported rock phosphate
and sulfur since the country does not have reserves of its own. However,
TSP trial production has just recently started on one plant, and the second
unit will commence trial production in February 1975 upon delivery of suit-
able Florida phosphate rock.
2.02      A description of the past performance of BFCPC's fertilizer enter-
prises and the steps being taken for improving their operations is given in
Annex 2-1. The Government has agreed to use its best efforts to take all
actions required to improve the p:-oduction of the existing fertilizer plants
at Chorasal, Chittagong and Fench iganj, taking into account the recommendations
made by the Tennessee Valley Auth;,rity (TVA) as part of its fertilizer marketing
- distribution study (para 3.11).  IDA will be consulted and will Z'ollow
progress in this regard. In addi':ion, the Government has agreed to employ a
consultant to assist BFCPC in establishing a uniform system of inventory
control for spare parts and raw miterials for all of its fertilizer plants.
Such a system is to be established by June 30, 1976 and should help eliminate
a major cause of present low plani: utilization. As further evidence of positive
action in this area, the Governmenrt has also agreed to allocate adequate
foreign exchange for spare parts znd raw materials, and IDA's Third Imports
Credit (Credit No. 515-BD) provides some financing for imports of spare parts,
sulfur and phosphate rock during fiscal year 1974/75.  With specifc reference
to the Chorasal plant, in 1972, tf.e Government engaged the servicec of Toyo
Engineering (Japan) to repair and rehabilitate the factory, and its problems
identified by Toyo, were to be corrected during the plant's first Echeduled
maintenance overhaul in 1974; but, as mentioned in the previous paragraph,
in the Fall of that year Ghorasal had to be shut down.  On the strength of
arrangements made in Japan for the supply of replacement equipment, BFCPC
hopes now to have the plant back in operation in May 1975, when it is expected
to operate close to full capacity. The Government is also considering
proposals to rebuild the Fenchuganj plant with foreign assistance; hlowever,
since the factory is nearing the ead of its useful life the extent of such
rehabilitation will first require careful study.
2.03      After the war of indeoenience BFCPC was established under the Bang-
ladesh Industrial Enterprises (Nationalization) Order of 1972 to "control,
supervise and coordinate" enterpr±3es established in the chemical anid pharma-
ceutical fields by EPIDC.  In add-:ion to the fertilizer plants referred to
in the two preceding paragraphs, al3out 48 enterprises, some abandorned by



-3-
their previous Pakistani owners, have been put under BFCPC's umbrella. A
brief description of BFCPC 's present status, functions and performance is
given in Annex 2-2.
B.   Corporate Structure and Ownership
2.04      Since BFCPC is already responsible for a large number of enterprises
and the proposed project will be a major unidertaking, the Government decided
ithat the project will be carried out by a new company, the Ashuganj Fertilizer
and Chemical Company Ltd. (AFCC), which was established October 16, 1974
under the Company's Act of 1913. Until the new company is adequately staffed
and becomes operational BFCPC will continue to act for it in further project
preparation and implementation with a full transfer of responsibilities and
commitments to be completed by mid-1975. However, BFCPC will transfer to
AFCC all rights and obligations relative to the land for the plant site7
the site development contract (para 4.09) and BFCPC's existing contract covering
the provision of technical advisory services for the project (para 2.06)
before the IDA credit becomes effective. Once the new company is fully
equipped to manage its affairs, BFCPC will restrict itself to the supervisory
and coordinating functions assigned to it under the 1972 Nationalization
Order, to be exercised through its nominees on the Board of DIrectors.  The
Government confirmed during negotiations its intention that BFCPC will have
a minority representation on the Board of Directors.  The Government will be
the sole shareholder of AFCC. The authorized share capital is Tk 1 billion
(Tk 100 crores) equivalent to US$134 million. Paid up capital (some US$107
million) will be represented by the Governiment's contributions to the cost
of the project, for which AFCC will issue shares to the Government.
C.   Organization and Management
2.05      The Company will have a Board of Directors of not less than three
and not miore than seven members, including the Chairman, all of whom will
be nominated by the Government but two of these shall be proposed to the
Government by B?CPC. Initially, a five member board is planned. The Directors,
with the exception of the Managing Director, will have no executive functions.
BFCPC has proposed a Chairman (the present BFCPC Chairman) and one Director,
and the Government has nominated two more Directors. The Managing Director
will be the Chief Executive of the Company. The Government has nominated
Mr. Momahad Yusuf, formerly the Member for Technology in the Bangladesh Atomic
Energy Commission, for thiis position. Mr. Yusuf has an engineering background
and about 22 years experience including power project management. The lenders
had an opportunity to review this nomination. The Government has also agreed
to consult with the lenders on the appointment of the Chairman and the
Managing Director should a vacancy occur in these positions; the same pro-
cedure would apply to the appointment of the Plant Manager and the Chief
Financial Officer of AFCC. The Company's Articles of Association has been
registered and is satisfactory; it gives the Company sufficient authority
to manage its affairs and operate the plant in keeping with sound business
prCnciples. Tt was further agreed that the by-laws and regulations of AFCC
will be submitted to the lenders for clearance prior to adoption and any



-4-
subsequent changes which could materially affect the operations of the
Company will require approval of the lenders.
2.06.     Following IDA guideLines, BFCPC in December 1973, selected a
Technical Advisor for the project consisting of a joint venture of the
Scientific Design Company Ltd. (UK) and James Chemical Engineering (US)
which is presently helping to organize a project team to implement the
project.  The team will subsequently be transferred to AFCC and on comple-
tion of the project assume plant operating functions.
2.07      Substantial outside assistance will be needed during the implemen-
tation phase and the early years of operation and expatriates will assume
in-line responsibility under the general direction of AFCC's Board of
Directors to achieve capacity output in the shortest possible time and to
maintain it thereafter. The scope and role of such management assistance
both during project implementation and initial operations has been agreed
in principle with the Government and the Company. Funds have been included
in the project cost estimate t:o pay for such assistance. Details are given
in para. 4.05.
III. FERTILIZER DEMAND, MARKETING AND DISTRIBUTION
3.01      Fertilizer use in Bangladesh has grown rapidly.  Annex 3-1, dis-
cusses the agricultural sector and the role fertilizer plays in Bangladesh's
agricultural strategy. Annex 3-2 provides historical consumption and supply
data as well as a comparison of fertilizer use in Bangladesh with that in
other countries. Annex 3-3 describes the present system for marketing and
distribution of fertilizers. The following paragraphs summarize the major
findings of these Annexes.
A.  Past Fertilizer Use
3.02      The following table shows the historical development of fertilizer
consumption, local supply and imports in Bangladesh since 1963/64.

The above table indicates the rapid growth of fertilizer use in Bangladesh
increasing from 47,900 nutrient tons in 1963/64 to 143,200 tons in 1970/71,
or by about 17% per year. The 1971 war led to disruption of both local pro-
duction and imports causing consumption to drop significantly, but in 1972/73
local production and consumption picked up sharply despite the scarcity and
significant rise in the local price of fertilizer. While the sharp increase
in local production of urea in 1972/73 allowed a major reduction in import
dependence, overall nutrient imports in that year still accounted for more
than half of consumption, and total available nitrogen production capacity
in Bangladesh was only utilized at about 45%. With the exception of relatively
small amounts of ammonium sulphate local production is in the form of urea.
The table also shows that production of nitrogen fertilizer has been rather
erratic and although highest in 1973/74 could not meet demand which, due to
a Government decision not to import urea, was artificially depressed.
3.03      The rapid recovery of fertilizer consumption in 1972/73 -- which
occurred in spite of the disruptions caused by the war, a severe drought
and the overall poverty of the farmers -- indicates the strength and resili-
ence of fertilizer demand. High and rising prices for rice and the spread
of high-yielding, fertilizer-responsive rice crops have contributed to the
growth of fertilizer demand, which was particularly strong in such progres-
sive agricultural areas, as Chittagong, Comilla, Dacca, and Kishorganj where
the use of these varieties has spread most rapidly. Urea is particularly



- 6 -
suitable for application on high--yielding rice varieties, not only for
agronomical reasons but also on account of its high analysis and the resulting
lower transport costs.
3.04      Although the farmers irL Bangladesh are increasingly aware of the
advantages of fertilizers and have augmented their use dramatically, they
still use only about 19 kg. of nuatrient per hectare of arable land.  This
dosage is on a par with other countries in the sub-continent, but is not
only very low considering the cropping intensity, water availability and
the levels of fertilizer use which would provide optimal yields but also
represents a mere 28% of the world average. It appears that neither the
lack of adequate agricultural credit institutions nor the small size of
farms have prevented fertilizer demand from growing even faster but that
adequacy of supply has been the major limiting factor, particularly in
recent years. Thus, while official prices of fertilizer at the farm level
have more than doubled (for urea from Tk 536 (US$71.5) per ton in 1972/73
to Tk 804 (US$107) in July 1973 to Tk 1,341 (US$179) in April 1974), there
were widespread reports of a fertilizer black market in 1973 and 1974 charging
two to three times the official price. Also a nationwide agricultural survey
in 1967/68 indicated that farmers with less than 2.5 acres used 35% more
fertilizer per acre than did farmers with larger farms. While up to date
figures are not available, indicaltions are that this is still so today.
B.   Forecast of Fertilizer Demand
3.05      The growth of fertilizer demand in Bangladesh will both determine,
and be determined by, the pace and structure of agricultural development in
Bangladesh. As described in Annex 3-1, the most promising opportunities for
increasing agricultural output over the next five to ten years lie in the
spread of high-yielding crop varieties and complementary inputs, particularly
fertilizer. An IDA financed Cereal Seeds Project (410-BD of June 29, 1973)
is expected to accelerate the ava-ilability and wider use of new rice and
wheat varieties.  Detailed fertil:Lzer demand projections through 1982/83 have
been prepared based on present farming practices, probable increases in ir-
rigated and rainfed areas, expected pricing policy (for agricultural inputs
and crops), and improvement in marketing and distribution during the forecast
period. The details and underlyirLg assumptions are given in Annex 3-2, and
are summarized below for urea, together with projections of local production.
These demand projections were prepared as part of the recent Basic Economic
Report on Bangladesh 1/ by an agrcnomist with long experience in the country's
agriculture.  Nevertheless, all such projections depend on a wide range of
interdependent assumptions and should be treated more as an indication of
the probable orders of magnitude involved rather than as precise predictions.
1/ Bangladesh: Development in a Rural Economy (in three volumes), IBRD
Report No. 455-BD, Grey Cover, July 31, 1974.

3.06      The projections imply that demand for urea would grow at an average
of about 12.3% a year over 1972/73, as compared with an actual annual consump-
tion growth of 15.4% over the past ten years. Accounting for anticipated
shutdowns of the two existing urea plants for extensive overhauling in 1974/
75, including repairs necessitated by the explosion in the Ghorasal plant
(para 2.01), and assuming 80% capacity utilization of these plants in
subsequent years, local demand will exceed local production until 1978/79,
the first year of production for the proposed Ashuganj plant (the Project).
There will then be about four years of modest surplus production before de-
mand again would exceed local supply, assuming that the project operates at
90% of installed capacity from 1980/81 onward.
3.07      No difficulty is expected in selling any such modest temporary
surplus on the world market. Since natural gas is now substantially cheaper
than other feedstocks for the production of nitrogen fertilizer, Bangladesh
should be able to export urea at prices both competitive and profitable.
India would be the most likely export market, given Bangladesh's proximity
and given the projected gap in India between local nitrogen fertilizer pro-
duction and demand. The Government, based on a feasibility study jointly
undertaken by BFCPC and the Fertilizer Corporation of India, is presently
pursuing plans to establish another ammonia/urea plant of similar capacity,
whose output India is prepared to take. However, even assuming that this
project would proceed without delay, India's gap between supply and demand
is expected to exceed one million tons of urea in 1979, so that there should
be no problem exporting whatever surplus urea Bangladesh can make available
to India alone.



- 8 -
C.   Marketing, Fertilizer Pricing and Distribution
3.08      Annex 3-3 describes in detail the system of marketing and distri-
bution of fertilizers, fertilizer pricing policy and the related subjects
of sales promotion, extension services and credit. The Bangladesh Agricul-
tural Development Corporation (BADC), a public sector corporation responsible
to the Ministry of Agriculture, procures (either locally or by import) and
distributes nearly all fertilizer used in Bangladesh down to the Thana
(county) level. 1/ There fertilizer is purchased either by one of the
29,000 private licensed retail dealers or, in the case of 62 of the 413
Thanas, by the Thana Central Cooperative Association (TCCA), which acts as
a wholesale agent and resells :he fertilizer either to its member village
cooperatives or to private retail agents.
3.09      Considering the inher:ent difficulties of transportation in Bangla-
desh, this fertilizer distribution system has worked rather well in the past.
It has proven flexible enough t:o handle the substantial expansion in fer-
tilizer use over the last decade. It has also, through the vast network of
small dealers, brought fertili::er to within the reach of virtually every
farmer in Bangladesh. The dealers as a matter of course sell fertilizer in
minute quantities -- often only a few kilograms at a time -- so that a
farmer can buy the precise amotut he wants and can afford. This is an
important advantage in a land cf small farms.
3.10      During the past few years, however, due to the rapid increase in
volume, price distortions, disruption of the transportation system and
severe general fertilizer shortages, the system has shown faults and attracted
increasing criticism. Many factors are involved in this. The difference
between the Indian and Bangladesh fertilizer prices -- Indian prices are
considerably higher -- has provided a strong incentive for smuggling and
for reasons mentioned previously (para 3.04) an active black market in
fertilizer has emerged. There also appear to be weak links within the
distribution system itself. As a consequence of all these factors, farmers
frequently complain that fertilizers are not available to the extent and
when they want them.
3.11      These problems, combined with the need to develop a system capable
of distributing much greater quantities of fertilizer in the future, have
led the Government to undertake a comprehensive fertilizer marketing and
distribution study. The main objective of the study is to propose insti-
tutional, organizational and policy improvements and to identify investment
needs in fertilizer distribution and marketing. The first phase of this
study, carried out by TVA and financed by USAID, began in April 1974
with the scope of work formulated in consultation with IDA. The TVA final
1/   The only exceptions are small amounts of fertilizer distributed to tea
estates and sugar mill estates by the Bangladesh Tea Association and
the Bangladesh Sugar Mills Corporation.



-9-
report is expected early 1975. IDA, in consultation with the Govern-
ment is formulating detailed terms of reference for the study's second phase
to be completed by late 1975, which would in turn develop necessary projects
in the fields of distribution and marketing, suitable for financing by an
external lender. Agreement on the scope and detail of the second phase of
the marketing-distribution study is a condition of credit effectiveness.
Among the objectives of the studies are the preparation of detailed area-wise
demand projections, analysis of bagging and handling methods, assessment of
storage needs, as well as review of transportation requirements, making use
of the recent Bangladesh Transport Survey, undertaken with assistance from
the UK Government. Financing of the foreign exchange cost of the study's
second phase is being arranged with a request from the Government to utilize
part of the IDA Technical Assistance Credit (Credit No. 409 BD).
3.12      The study will also review fertilizer pricing policy.  As de-
scribed in Annex 3-3, fertilizers for years have been sold to farmers at
heavily subsidized prices, primarily to promote their use. Recognizing
that farmers are now aware of the advantages of fertilizer and that demand
for fertilizer is strong, and concerned about the Government's financial
resource situation and about reports that the farmers with larger farms
benefitted more from the subsidies than smaller farmholders, the first
Five-Year Plan (1973/74-1977/78) called for a phased reduction in subsidies.
The soaring costs of imported fertilizer following publication of the Plan
added another dimension to the problem and prompted the Government to raise
fertilizer prices sharply (para 3.04). The present pricing structure no
longer subsidizes the sale of locally produced urea. Prices of imported
fertilizers, on the other hand, are still subsidized in varying degrees,
but the subsidy is likely to be eliminated or substantially reduced as
world market prices for fertilizer return to more normal levels after the
next several years and as the Government continues to carry out its policy,
which was reiterated during negotiations, of a phased reduction of subsidies.
3.13      The fertilizer marketing and distribution study will also investi-
gate the area of sales promotion in its broadest sense, including extension
services and credit. As fertilizer supplies come into better balance with
demand, the present sellers' market situation is likely to change. In addi-
tion, as farmers move somewhat closer to optimal fertilizer dosages they
will require more accurate information on fertilizer use and its effect on
various crops. This would suggest an increasingly important role for ex-
tension and demonstration activities in the future. While these activities
(Annex 3-3), are now inadequate, the Government, together with an FAO/IBRD
cooperative program team is working on the preparation of a Rural Development
Education and Training Project, for possible financing by IDA, which could
constitute an important first step in the long range improvement of extension
and farmer training.
3.14      An effective system of agricultural credit, capable of providing
farmers with timely finance on reasonable terms, could ease the financial
burden to farmers of increasingly intensive fertilizer use. As discussed
in the recent Bank Economic Report on Bangladesh (IBRD No. 455-BD) and in



- 10 -
Annex 3-3, the agricultural credit institutions in the country now suffer
from serious weaknesses. There 1las been a dialogue between the Government
and IDA on ways and means for improving these institutions, but it is
clear that efforts to improve the system of agricultural credit in Bangladesh
will require careful development and will need time. The fertilizer demand
projections have taken into consideration the limitations of the existing
credit system, and lack of farmer credit is therefore not expected to hold
demand below projected levels. Government credit now plays a very limited
role in fertilizer purchase since only about 15% of farmers' borrowings come
from government sources. Moreover, only a small part of a farmer's borrowings
is used for purchasing fertilizer and only a fraction of all fertilizer sales
are financed by credit. Limited reliance on existing credit sources, combined
with growing use of high-yielding varieties and continued profitable rice
prices, should enable demand to expand at the rate projected.
3.15      The Government has agreed to review the findings of the first and
second phases of the fertilizer marketing and distribution study with the
lenders and promptly take the necessary action to eliminate physical and
institutional constraints which may arise as the volume of fertilizer to
be distributed grows. This review should provide the basis for an understanding
between the Government and the lenders on the content and the objective of
the anticipated marketing and distribution project and permit completion of
such project within the overall time frame of the proposed Ashuganj urea
manufacturing project.
IV. IHE PROJECT
A.  Project Scope
4.01      The project will be located at Ashuganj some 60 km northeast of
Dacca and will consist of a 1,600 TPD urea plant with an intermediate ammonia
production plant of 925 TPD, and all necessary ancillary facilities. The
ammonia plant will be a single train unit and the urea plant will also be
single train if future bids prove this to be the most economic design. On
the basis of 330 on-stream days per year the plant will have annual capacities
of approximately 528,000 tons urea and 305,000 tons ammonia. These are
standard size capacities and the project will employ modern commercially
proven technology to ensure efficient operation. The production processes
selected and the plant facilities provided are described in Annexes 4-1 and
4-2. The site area is about 130 acres of which 65 acres will be occupied by
the plant, leaving another 65 acres for a housing compound for the employees
and common facilities. The site is on the eastern bank of the Meghna River
and has good rail and water connections. The town of Bhairab Bazaar and the
Ashunganj power station are nearby (Annex 4-3, IBRD Map 11075).
B.   Raw Materials and Utilities
4.02      The principal raw material will be natural gas supplied from the
existing Titas field, some 12 km from the project site. The Bangladesh



- 11 -
Shell Oil Company Ltd., which is 25% owned by the Government, operates the
fi ld and has determined the total reserves at about 63 billion cubic meters
(m ). Details of the gas reserves for the major gasfields in Bangladesh
including Titas, and gas analyses are given in Annex 4-4. The gas is es-
pecially suitable for amnonia production containing some 97% methane with
negligible amounts of sulfur. The gas offtake projections for the Titas
field are shown in Annex 4-5. Even assuming that gas demand would con-
tinue to increase at about 11% per year predicted for the remainder of the
decade, the Titas gas reserves would last for more than 30 years.
4.03      An existing high pressure gas transmission line operated by the
Titas Gas Transmission and Distribution Company Ltd. (TGTDC), a wholly
Government-owned entity, passes within about 2 km of the project site and
presently has an unused capacity of some 2.6 million cubic meters per day
(m3PD) or more than double the project's requirements. Prior to the pro-
posed credit becoming effective, the Government will ensure that a satis-
factory quantity of gas (approximately 8 billion m3) from the Titas field
will be dedicated to the project, and AFCC will enter into a long term gas
supply contract with TGTDC on terms and conditions satisfactory to the
lenders. The contract will obligate TGTDC to construct a gas pipeline to
the factory perimeter.
4.04      The nearby Ashuganj power station operated by the Power Develop-
ment Board (PDB) has adequate capacity to serve the project; however, a
study is now being made by AFCC's Technical Advisors (para 4.07) to determine
whether greater reliability and improved economies may be achieved by in-
cluding an on-site power generation facility in the project.  In the meantime
provision has been made in the project cost estimate for inclusion of such
facility. In the event that an on-site power plant is not included in the
project, AFCC will enter into a contract with the PDB for the supply of
power, to be satisfactory to the lenders, prior to December 31, 1975. Water
for industrial and drinking purposes, will be obtained from the Meghna River.
The supply of all utilities is thus satisfactorily assured.
C.   Employment, Training and Management Assistance
4.05      The project will provide employment for about 1,200 persons.  Of
these some 525 will be key technical, maintenance and operational personnel
distributed over ammonia production (116), urea production (112), maintenance
department (260), and laboratory (37). This complement of key personnel will
be developed during the project's construction period by drawing where
possible suitable individuals from within the BFCPC enterprises, particularly
the four existing fertilizer works, and training them to the specific re-
quirements of the project. The training programs will initially be devised
by AFCC's Technical Advisor in close cooperation with the Engineering Firm
for the project (para 4.07) and include intensive training abroad for selected
staff in plants of similar type. It will be the responsibility of the Engi-
neering Firm (para 4.08) to make the necessary arrangements for this foreign
training. Additionally, a team of expatriate experts will be appointed by
AFCC to assist with the administrative and general supervision of the Company



- 12 -
during the construction stage of the project and during the first 2 or 3
years of commercial production. This management assistance will also
include training of the Company's Bengalee management and technical staff.
An amount of US$6.6 million foreign exchange to cover its cost has been
included in the project cost estimate and the general scope of services
to be provided is outlited in Arnex 4-6.  Prior to credit effectiveness,
AFCC will make arrangements, including an implementation plan satisfactory
to the lenders, to obtain the services of experts to fill advisory, technical
and managerial positions within the Company for which qualified Bengalees are
not available and to train Bengalees to fill such positions. AFCC is
soliciting proposals for these services in accordance with IDA Guidelines.
D.   Ecology
4.06      There are presently no statutory regulations in Bangladesh for
industrial effluents, and the project will be consequently designed in ac-
cordance with European and U.S. standards in respect to solid, liquid and
gaseous emissions. As the natural gas from the Titas field to be used
as raw material and fuel is virtually sulfur free, atmospheric pollution
problems are expected to be minimal. Liquid effluent from the plant will
be treated in waste water treatment units prior to discharge into the Meghna
River, and, under these circumstances, the adverse effect of the project on
the environment is expected to be negligible. Pollution control facilities
forming about 2% of project costs are satisfactory.
E.   Project Execution
4.07      The groups responsible for project execution will be (a) the
Company (AFCC) which will be the owner and executing authority and which
will include (b) the Management Assistance Team whose members will assume
line positions within AFCC; (c) the Technical Advisor already selected
(para 2.06), who will act as Consultant to AFCC throughout the course of
the project from preparation of specifications and tender documents to
final acceptance of the complete plant and (d) the Engineering Firm who
will contract with AFCC for the fsupply of the complete plant on a turnkey
responsibility basis. Bids are b,eing sought on a fixed price basis for
provision of process licenses and all services, with all equipment,
materials, freight and construction costs being reimbursable at cost.
The initial services of the Technical Advisor up to the date the IDA credit
becomes effective which is expect:ed to follow shortly after award of the
Engineering Firm contract is being financed by part of the US$4.0 million
IDA Technical Assistance Credit RCredit No. 409 BD) which was approved by
the Executive Directors on June 28, 1973. US$400,000 has been appropriated
to cover this period. There is no plan for refinancing of this upon approval
of the project credit.
4.08      The Engineering Firm wlll have full responsibility for imple-
menting the project, including the provision of process licenses, basic
and detailed engineering and the management of procurement, construction,
start-up and commissioning. Inv:itations to bid for the Engineering Firm



- 13 -
contract have been issued to four firms which have been prequalified in
accordance with the Association's-Guidelines and bids are expected by
February 1975. Equipment and materials procurement will be conducted from
the Engineering Firm's offices with participation by AFCC and its Technical
Advisor. The project-completion date, which is defined as the date of
acceptance of the total plant after successful completion of all performance
tests, is scheduled for the end of July 1978, as shown in Annex 4-7. 1/
4.09      Detailed civil engineering design will be undertaken by the
Engineering Firm and civil construction by local contractors or by local
labor directly hired by the Engineering Firm who will supervise the work
in either case.  The plant will be designed to withstand winds of
typhoon velocity which are common in Bangladesh. Preparatory survey and
engineering work has been undertaken by a competent and experienced local
consulting engineering firm and the contract for dredging and filling the
site has been awarded to a US firm after international competitive bid-
ding. The site will be filled and raised by an average of 20 feet which
would protect the plant from the seasonal monsoon-floods. Plant erection
will be undertaken mainly by local labor directly hired by the Engineering
Firm supplemented by skilled expatriate labor as required. AFCC will form
a project team from its own staff and that of the Management Assistance Firm
to oversee the construction of the plant and all ancillary facilities.
V. CAPITAL COSTS AND FINANCING PLAN

been provided from an IDA technical assistance credit (para 4.07). The
financing still required therefore is US$249.0 million of which US$141.9
million is foreign exchange. The estimate is summarized on the following
page and shown in more detail in Annex 5-1. This estimate initially was
prepared jointly by the project's Technical Advisor and the IDA appraisal
mission but is now based on price levels prevailing in September 1974; it
takes account of equipment price increases in major supplying countries due
to the worldwide inflation and equipment supply shortage. Physical
contingencies provided are 9.6% 2/ for both local and foreign base cost

estimates (BCE) and are based on the detailed contingency schedule included
in Annex 5-1. Price escalation provided is 30% and 17% of local and foreign
BCE and physical contingencies respectively. Escalation rates for equipment
have been taken at 14% for 1974, 11% for 1975 and 7.5% for 1976 onward, and
for civil works at 18% for 1974, 15% for 1975 and 12% for 1976 onward, all
as detailed in Annex 5-1. These rates are considered reasonable because the
basic estimate being founded on September 1974 price levels already includes
a substantial price escalation component attributable to scarcity factors.
Ordering of equipment is expected to commence by the fourth quarter of 1975 1/
and to be substantially completed by the third quarter of 1976. Import duty
at an average rate of 40% has been allowed. The estimate assumes a project
implementation period of 52 months, inclusive of 4 months testing and
commissioning, dating from April 1, 1974 when appraisal was undertaken. The
project may be deemed to have commenced, however, in December 1973 with the
appointment of the Technical Advisor.
5.02      Accurate projections of price escalation are very difficult under
current conditions, even more so for equipment for the fertilizer and re-
lated industries which are expanding rapidly throughout the world. Inability
to obtain materials on time, longer delivery periods, or even lack of an ade-
quate response to requests for bids could all lead to higher foreign exchange
costs than estimated. However, to try to provide a reserve against all con-
ceivable levels of price increases would be counter-productive because such
provision, which may prove to be unnecessary, could lead to a loss of re-
sources for other projects in Bangladesh, given the limited annual allocation
of concessional finance by all lenders, and could relax tight cost control.
Consequently, while the above project cost estimates are considered realistic
and are well in excess of previous estimates for similar projects now under
construction, the possibility of a cost increase and the need for additional
financing, though small, cannot be ruled out altogether.
5.03      The initial working capital requirements in addition to the spare
parts and catalysts included in the equipment cost estimate, are estimated
at Tk 96 million with a foreign exchange component of Tk 14 million
(US$1.9 million) as shown in Annex 5-2. The foreign exchange component
is included in the foreign financing package, while the local currency
component will be provided by the Government from its own resources as part
of its equity contribution (as shown in the financing plan).

equity contribution will cover local expenditures while the foreign loans
will cover the estimated foreign currency costs. It is shown below:

will be permitted to inject a portion of the IDA credit as equity or to
extend a portion of local currency as loan as the case may be, to maintain
the debt equity ratio close to, but not in excess of 60/40. Should there
be any cost increase the obligaticn to find the additional funds needed
to complete the project will be with the Government.
1/   The Government's commitment to provide equity has been covered in a draft
Financing Agreement with AFCC, acceptable to the lenders, and annual
provisions for amounts needed will be made in the development budget.



- 17 -
5.05      All foreign loans will be made to the Government on concessional
terms for a weighted average period and interest rate of 39 years and about
1.0% per annum, and will be passed on to the AFCC on the same terms as the
proceeds of the IDA credit (Annex 5-3).  The proceeds of :he IDA credit are
to be extended from the Government to AFCC at 10% effective annual interest
for 15 years including 5 years of grace and equal semi-annual principal pay-
ments with the Company bearing the foreign exchange risk during this period.
The standard rate used by the Government when lending to state-owned enter-
prises is 7-1/4%. In response to the Association's position that no rate
below 10% - the minimum rate currently used for industrial projects - would
be acceptable, the Government and the other lenders agreed to a 10% onlending
rate. With this rate, total interest during construction is estimated at
Tk 204 million (US$27.2 million) as per Annex 5-4, based on the disbursement
schedule set forth in Annex 5-5.
C.  Procurement and Allocation of Foreign Funds
5.06      Approximately 66% of the foreign funds available for the project
will be untied with the balance being tied to procurement in the USA or
developing countries (21%), and the UK (13%). In order to ensure most
economic use of these funds and for ease and speed of administration, the
foreign exchange financing will, in part, be on a parallel basis with
separate specific lists of goods and services being allocated to ADB
(US$30.0 million, partly tied); AID (US$20.7 million, tied) 1/; ODM (US$11.7
million, tied); and KfW (US$12 million, untied). An explanation of the
rationale behind this preallocation and details of the items preallocated
are given in Annex 5-6. The funds of IDA, GOI and GOS (US$51.9 million)
will be used to finance, in accordance with IDA Guidelines, services,
equipment, and the foreign exchange portion of civil works not preallocated
or remaining after tied funds have been exhausted. The imiost likely major
item to fall in this category will be the contract for the Engineering Firm
estimated at US$24.5 million including provision of a bonus for early
completion. It is expected that the remaining tied funds of AID (US$9.3
million) and ODM (US$6.3 million) can be used to finance contracts won by
US or UK suppliers respectively under international competitive bidding
(ICB). However, should it appear that some of the tied funds cannot be
utilized in this manner, then additional equipment and services will be
allocated by IDA, in consultation with the lender with the remaining tied
funds, for procurement through competitive bidding within the territories
acceptable to that lender. With the allocation procedures described above,
it is anticipated that about 83% of the foreign funds will be utilized on
the basis of either ICB or approved international shopping lists. The
allocation of the foreign loan funds would then be as follows:
1/   US$8.1 million of this amount has been allocated for the dredging
contract (para 4.09) which was placed under ICB. Hence, only
US$12.6 million remains as tied funds.

a preference of 15% or the amolunt of the customs duty whichever is lower.
Items which are critical, eithLer technically or because of their limited
availability, may be subject to restricted bidding among qualified suppliers
approved by the lender concerned and are expected to total between US$10 and
13 million. Most of these items, however, will be equipment for the ammonia
and urea plants which are either not being financed by IDA or only to a very
minor extent (urea plant). The upper limit of the value of critical items
which may be financed by IDA, therefore, is unlikely to exceed US$3 million.
As to construction services, although significant awards to local construction
firms are not anticipated, such local firms participating in ICB will, for
purposes of bid evaluation, receive a preference of 7-1/2%, and for contracts
won by them in competition with foreign firms, 35% of the contract value
(deemed to be the foreign exchange component) will be eligible for financing
out of unallocated, untied funds including IDA. The aggregate foreign
exchange financing for such local contracts is not expected to exceed US$4
million and will probably be considerably less. It is anticipated that
sub-contracts related to the site dredging and filling work (para 4.09) awarded
after November 1, 1974 but prior to IDA credit effectiveness, amounting to

about US$0.6 million equivalent, will not qualify for USAID financing.
Consequently, retroactive financing by IDA up to US$0.6 million for these
sub-contracts may be necessary. IDA has reached agreement with the other
lenders on the above allocation of goods and services and procurement
procedures as well as on other matters related to project execution such
as coordinated project supervision missions.  Finally, the Governmentts
procedures and regulations relevant to the approval of contracts, the
issuance of import licenses, application of import duties, and inland
transportation to the site are discussed in Annex 5-7. On all these, speedy
action by the Government is essential to avoid project delays and specific
assurances have been received from the Government to this effect.
VI.  FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
A.  Financial Analysis of the Project
6.01      The financial analysis is based on a projection of 12 operating
years with the plant depreciated over the same period on a straight line
basis as shown in Annex 6-1, and a gradual buildup from the assumed start
of commercial operations (August 1978) to 90% capacity utilization in the
third operating year (1980). Operating costs and the sales price of urea
are those expected to prevail at plant start-up; they have been kept con-
stant throughout the forecast period. Except for the gas, operating costs
are assumed to be about 40 to 50% higher than those prevailing in 1974 and
the underlying assumptions are shown in Annexes 6-2 and 6-3. The gas con-
tract between AFCC and TGTDC specifies a price of Tk 3.72 (US$0.50) per
MSCF as set by the Government. This is about 60% higher than the recently
revised price to other consumers. 1/ However, the contract price is not
fixed but would change to reflect any changes in the Government's pricing
policy. The price of Tk 3.72 per MSCF is used in the financial projections.
6.02      The Government has agreed to set the ex-factory price at a level
that will provide at least a debt service coverage of 1.5 for the project
and yield a reasonable return on equity. A minimum ex-factory price of
US$155/ton of urea would meet these conditions and has been used in the
financial projections. This price is about 50% higher than the early 1974
ex-factory price for the existing urea plants, but is low relative to the
expected 1978 ex-factory prices that these plants will charge as a result of
rising production costs, especially for spare parts, chemicals and possibly
heavy reconditioning costs.
1/   The gas price was US$0.21 per MSCF during 1973 and was raised to
US$0.31 in mid-1974. The Government sets the gas price and only one
price is used for all users, irrespective of distance transmitted and
volume consumed.

6.04      The capital intensive nature of the project is evident from the
cost structure. Depreciation charges and maintenance materials account for
about 56% of production costs as against 17.2% for natural gas and 7.9% for
labor expenses. This aspect -- where capital charges are very much more
dominant than feedstock costs -- provides some cushion against feedstock
price escalations. The projections assume that urea will be bagged in heavy,
single film bags made by the Company from imported polyethylene chips.
Although international prices for these chips have escalated rapidly during
the recent year, a 60% import tariff on them is primarily responsible for
the high cost of bagging materials.
6.05      It has not yet been firmly decided whether heavy wall plastic
bags or plastic lined jute bags will be used by the project, and a detailed
analysis will be undertaken by the marketing consultants. An estimate of
the cost of producing heavy wall plastic bags (which have been assumed in
the project's production cost estimates) is given in Annex 6-4. Although
the cost of plastic lined jute bags would be considerably higher, their use
could conceivably be justified on the basis of their superior handling
characteristics and the use of locally produced jute. If plastic lined jute
bags or any other alternative is ultimately recommended, the Government has
agreed to increase the ex-factory price of urea to reflect the difference
in bagging costs; this also follows from the debt service criterion for
setting the project's ex-factory price.

6.07      The project is expected to incur an operating loss during the first
5 months of operations because of the assumed capacity utilization of 50%,
but should operate profitably from then on. The debt service coverage is
about 1.5 times during the third year as full debt repayment commences and
increases gradually thereafter. The operating cash requirement of the
Company is determined by a minimum current ratio of 1.5 and dividend payments
are not assumed in the projections. The accumulated cash in excess of

about 2% each year thereafter. The cash breakeven point is initially 34%
but increases to 51% during the third year, as full debt repayments commence,
and declines also by 2% each subsequent year. Annex 6-7 discusses the fi-
nancial analysis in more detail.

6.11      The financial rate of return is low relative to similar projects
for the following reasons: (a) conservative (long) construction period;
(b) high capital costs on account of import duties and infrastructure (site)
development; (c) low ex-factory price; (d) the high corporate tax rate;
(e) the assumption that surplus funds are not reinvested in revenue-earning
assets.
C.   Financial Covenants
6.12      To ensure sound financial management AFCC has agreed to the fol-
lowing covenants: the Company will (a) not incur additional long term debt
if its debt/equity ratio would exceed 60:40 or if its debt service coverage
ratio will fall below 1.5 times at any time; (b) maintain a minimum current
ratio of 1.5:1 with dividend payments or other cash distributions to be
made out of accumulated earnings and subject to the above current ratio
test; and (c) not undertake any capital investment program in excess of
US$3 million equivalent per year during the initial three years of commercial
production without the prior approval of IDA.
D.    Major Risks
6.13      There are major risks involved in the project:  further equipment
price escalation in a strong sellers' market beyond that assumed; delays
in equipment delivery from fully booked suppliers; and most importantly,
construction delays due to administrative inertia and low capacity utilization
due to ineffective management or operating standards as presently experienced
in Bangladesh's existing fertilizer factories. The assumptions on construction



- 24 -
time and capacity use, while reasonable, are predicated on specific procedures
to accelerate Government action on project related requirements and effective
management assistance with major responsibilities in project implementation,
initial production and the financial and administrative functions of the
enterprise to be given to expatr:.ate experts. Lack of such effective
assistance could cost Bangladesh very dearly and is something the country
can not afford. However, as ment:ioned previously the Company and the
Government have agreed in principle on administrative procedures and assistance
arrangements satisfactory to IDA and the other lenders, and the risk is
considered acceptable.
6.14      Commercial risks in the sale and distribution of urea, including
the possible need for some temporary exports, are also present, but these
are considered comparatively mincr, since Bangladesh will have to utilize
most of the project's output if it wants to meet the food requirements of its
rapidly growing population.
VII. ECCNOMIC ANALYSIS
A.  World Fertilizer Prices
7.01      During the late 1960's, world fertilizer prices were depressed
due to excess supply and consequently, investments in new fertilizer capacity
were modest. When demand expanded rapidly during 1972-1973, it was not
matched with increased supply and prices have risen sharply, partly due
to scarcity and partly due to increases in hydrocarbon and phosphate rock
prices, the major raw materials far fertilizer production. Urea fertilizer
prices, for example, are now about US$300 per ton (f.o.b., bagged). Freight
charges, which also constitute a najor portion of imported fertilizer costs,
have also increased sharply. The present world-wide fertilizer shortage
and Bangladesh's severe foreign exchange constraint have made it difficult
for the country to rely on imports to a major extent to meet the growing
domestic demand. With ample and inderutilized natural gas resources,
domestic urea production has emerged as an economically attractive proposition
for Bangladesh.
B.   Economic Analysis
7.02      The economic analysis i;3 based on 1978 economic operating costs 1/
and 1978 urea import price (e.g. c.i.f. Chittagong) of US$170 per ton (bagged).
This is roughly composed of an equivalent f.o.b. (Persian Gulf or Indonesia)
urea price of US$145 per ton plus sea fright of US$25 per ton and is con-
sistent with the forecasts presently used by the Bank in other fertilizer
projects. Local port handling of US$5 per ton is added to arrive at the
economic price of US$175 per ton. No adjustments for inland freight are
1/   Details about the economic p:-oduction costs are given in Annex 6-3.



- 25 -
made since distribution costs from the port of entry and from the plant
site are expected to be of the same order of magnitude. Except after trans-
formation to urea fertilizer, it appears, as discussed in Annex 7-1, that
the natural gas resources of Bangladesh cannot be properly considered an
exportable commodity, either directly or indirectly. Bangladesh will not
import any crude oil or its derivatives to replace the natural gas used for
the project. Furthermore, almost all economically and technically feasible
substitution of gas for oil has already taken place. Assuming gas con-
sumption grows by 11% annually, present reserves will last in excess of
30 years. Consequently, the shadow price of the natural gas is taken to be
identical to the price used in the financial analysis. This yields an
economic price of US$0.50 per MSCF. 1/ Similarly, the labor rate used in
the financial projections is taken as the economic wage rate since most of
the labor employed in the project are skilled personnel and the wage bill
is not a large proportion of production costs. Import duties and interest
during construction are excluded from the capital costs which are converted
to constant mid-1978 dollars in Annex 6-8 for the economic analysis. Finally,
local costs are converted at the official foreign exchange rate of Tk 7.50
per US dollar and a twelve-year operating life is assumed.
C.   Economic Rate of Return and Sensitivity Analysis
7.03      The economic costs and benefit streams for the project are shown
in Annex 7-2; and the economic rate of return and sensitivity analyses in
Annex 7-3. The economic return is 21.2% and the sensitivity analysis shows
that should a shadow exchange rate of Tk 14.0 per US dollar be used to
reflect the scarcity of foreign exchange, the return improves to 25.0%.
On the other hand, if the natural gas shadow price is based on an energy
cost of US$1.40 per million BTU 2/ (equivalent to US$9-10 per barrel of crude
oil), on the assumption that the gas is a tradeable commodity, then the
rate of return falls to 17.8%. It is also sensitive to capital cost increases.
A 20% cost overrun reduces the return by 3.6 percentage points. The rate
of return is still satisfactory even under fairly adverse conditions, as
shown below together with cases when the economic price of urea is US$20 per
ton above and below the assumed price.
1/   Refer to Annex 7-1 for further details.
2/   Since the Titas gas has an average net hearing value of 938 BTU per
MSCF, the shadow price would be US$1.31 per MSCF.

D.   Annual Foreign Exchange Savings and the Efficiency of Import Sub-stitution
7.04      The economic benefits Df the project are primarily in the form of
foreign exchange savings due to efficient import substitution. As shown in
Annex 7-4, when the project reaches 90% capacity utilization by the third full
operating year, the gross annual foreign exchange savings will be about
US$74 million in 1978 dollars, or US$155 per ton of urea produced by the
project based on a CIF price of IJS$170 per ton. This high level of foreign
exchange savings is reflected by the favorable economic rate of return of
21.2%. A -,ore precise indicator of Bangladesh's comparative cost advantage
in the prodwction of urea is the efficiency of import substitution, which as
shown in Ann,x 7-5, is about 0.5 implying that only US$0.50 of domestic
resource cos   are incurred by tile project to save US$1.00 in foreign
exchange.
VI'..  AGREEMENTS
8.01      Prior to or during r.ngctiations, agreements were reached on the
following major issues:
A.   Agreements with the Government
1.   The Government has agreed to use its best efforts to take
all action required to improve the production at the
existing plants, and as part of this program appoint or
cause the appointment of a consultant to assist in
establishing a spare parts inventory control system for
the existing fertilizer plants and will make every effort
to promptly implement tie recommendations arising from the
fertilizer marketing and distribution study (para 2.02);



- 27 -
2.   The Government will consult with the lenders on the
appointments of the Chairman and Managing Director of
AFCC (paras 2.04 and 2.05);
3.   The Government will review the findings of the fertilizer
marketing and distribution study with IDA and the other
lenders to determine ways of improving the marketing and
distribution of fertilizer, and ensure implementation of
the necessary project so identified within the overall time
frame of the Ashuganj urea manufacturing project. Agreement
on the scope and detail of the second phase of this study
is a condition of credit effectiveness (paras 3.11 and 3.15);
4.   The Government will ensure that approximately 8 billion
cubic meters of the natural gas resources of the Titas
field will be dedicated to the project and that a Gas
Purchase Agreement acceptable to the lenders will be entered
into between the AFCC and the TGTDC prior to credit effective-
ness. In particular, the Government will ensure that TGTDC
will construct in time as required by the project a gas
pipeline from the existing mainline to the AFCC factory
premises (para 4.03);
5.   The Government will provide equity funds to AFCC in time as
required by the project so that a minimum of 40% equity
participation is attained. In the event the local currency
cost of the project is less than 40% of the project cost,
the Government will invest part of the IDA credit as
equity to raise the equity participation to 40%. Should a
cost increase occur, the Government will provide the funds
needed to complete the project. The availability of the
other foreign loans is a condition of credit effectiveness
(para 5.04);
6.   The Government will relend all the proceeds of the foreign
loans to AFCC at a rate of 10% per annum, for 15 years
(starting December, 1974), including a 5 year grace period.
The Company will bear the foreign exchange risk (para 5.05);
7.   The Government will ensure prompt issuance of permits and
licenses, prompt customs clearance for imported project
material, the provision of sufficient inland transport facilities,
and the expeditious review and approval of project related
contracts where such Governmental approval is required
(para 5.07); and
8.   The Government will set the ex-factory price of urea (or
any other product of the Company) at a level sufficient to
allow AFCC to earn a reasonable return on equity, and
maintain a current ratio of 1.5:1 and a debt service coverage of
at least 1.5 times (paras 6.02 and 6.12).



- 28 -
B.   Agreements with AFCC or B1FCPC on Behalf of AFCC
1.   BFCPC will act for A1'CC in project implementation until
transfer to AFCC of p,roject responsibilities, assets and
commitments are completed. BFCPC shall transfer to AFCC
all rights and obligations relative to the plant site,
the site development contract and the Technical Advisor's
contract prior to credit effectiveness (para 2.04);
2.   AFCC will consult with the lenders prior to appointing
the Plant Manager and the Chief Financial Officer of the
Company (para 2.05);
3.   AFCC will enter into a Gas Purchase Agreement contract
with TGTDC on terms and conditions acceptable to the lenders
prior to credit effectiveness (para 4.03);
4.   If an on-site power pLant is not included in the project, AFCC
will contract with th- Power Development Board (PDB) by

acceptable to the lenders (para 4.04);
5.   Prior to credit effect:iveness, AFCC will make arrangements
to contract for management assistance during project
implementation and during the initial years of operation.
This contract and the whole training and operational
assistance program will be subject to the approval of the
lenders (paras 2.07 arnd 4.05); and
6.   AFCC will observe pruclent financial management and observe
the financial covenants contained in para 6.12.

with the Government and the lenders (para 5.06).
8.03      Based on the above agreements, the project is suitable for an
IDA credit of US$33 million equivalent.
