Page 5
9.        The increase in fertilizer prices was a necessary step, but it is
difficult to see the rationale for the structure of the price increases. The
new prices, in effect, tax urea to subsidize TSP and MP. Moreover, the
absolute size of the subsidy for fertilizers may grow, depending both on the
mix of fertilizers consumed by the farmers (particularly on how much TSP is
used) and on whether urea imports are necessary. Given the volatility of the
world market for fertilizers, it is difficult to determine what pricing
policies Bangladesh should follow to balance the current, short term, high
fertilizer prices against the longer term fertilizer prices which may be sub-
stantially lower. It is clear that the Government's financial position does
not allow it to continue to sell fertilizer at a loss indefinitely.  All of this
suggests the necessity of a careful review of fertilizer pricing policy.
C. Sales Promotion, Extension and Credit
10.       Fertilizer dealers have in the past several years faced a seller's
market.  With demand for fertilizers exceeding supply, little effort was
required to sell available stocks. With fertilizer use so low, the marginal
increase in output from using fertilizers was very high to the farmers. This,
combined with high and rising rice prices and the introduction of new, more
fertilizer-responsive, high-yielding varieties, has kept demand growing at a
brisk pace. In the future, supplies of fertilizer, particularly urea, are
likely to be in better balance with demand. Moreover, growth in demand for
fertilizer may slow down as farmers increase their dosages to levels closer 'o,
but still well below, their economic optimum. This may happen because the
farmer does not know what the economically optimum dose for his use is, because
the farmer is unwilling to take greater risks for smaller marginal gains in the
face of great uncertainty about floods, droughts and crop prices, and because
the farmer is unwilling to bear the greater financial burden implied by the
higher doses.   All this implies    that it may become more and more difficult
in the future to get farmers to use the fertilizers needed to keep agricultural
production growing.
11.       Sates promotion activities in their broadest sense will, therefore,
be increasingly neces6ry.  As noted above, the relatively small commission
earned by the average dealer is not likely to cause him to undertake any
major promotion effort. (Althuagh the commission has been raised from Tk 40
to Tk 60 per ton, in view of the parallel increase in fertilizer prices, the
retailer's commission has fallen in percentage terms.) The present system,
however, has the important advantages of bringing fertilizer almost to the
fanmer's doorstep and of selling fertilizers in the tiny quantities desired
by small farmers.
12.       Before the fertilizer dealers, or even the extension agents (Union
Agricultural Assistants or UAA's), can encourage farmers to use more fertilizer
or increase the proportion of P and K nutrients in the fertilizer mix farmers
use, they first must have some better notion of the economically optimal ferti-
lizer doses. As suggested above, most farmers view the present recommended
doses as unrealistically high. The recommendations are based on field trials
carried out in ways which make it impossible to deternine economically optimal
doses or the independent effects, over a range of doses, of the different major
nutrients (N, P and K). Moreover, they take no account of the changing relation-
ship between crop prices and fertilizer prices, of the risk and uncertainty



MIMIX 3-3
Pag,e 6
faced by the farmer, or of the considerable variations which exist in dif-
ferent parts of the country. Nor do the recommendations offer much advice as
to when and how to apply the fertilizer.  A necessary first step would be to
review past fertilizer trial data and begin a new series of trials desigl)ed
to provide information more meaningful for the farmers' needs.  Dual purpose
fertilizer trial/demonstration plots could produce the data needed and show
farmers the results at the same time.  Armed with such information - conveyed
either in a short course or through simple written instructions - the fertilizer
dealer could encourage greater fertilizer use based on sound technical advice.
13.       The existing channels of conveying technical information to the
farmers - the extension services and the farmer training programs at the Thana
Training and Development Centers - should play a major role in carrying the
results of the fertilizer trials and other tecimological improvements, to the
farmers.  Indeed, these channels could themselves, in coordination with the
relevant research   institutions organize and carry oult the trials and demon-
strations.  The extension serice and the farmer training programs currently
have serious weaknesses, however, which preclude relying strongly on them in
the short term.
14.       At present the main extension network consists of some 4,000 exten-
sion workers (Union Agricultural Assistants or UAA's) posted at the Union level-/
They are the only development officers posted at that low an administrative
level. Their training includes a secondary school pass and perhaps some training
at an Agricultural Extension Training Institute. They are each responsible for
the agricultural output of over 5,000 acres and for some 1,700 farm families.
They are expected to be knowledgable about the many varieties of the many crops
grown, the latest improvements in cultivation practices, fertilizer doses,
pesticides and their use, fisheries, livestock and irrigation. At the same
time they are supposed to meet production targets, gather statistics, help with
the census, assist in relief work and do anything else that comes to mind.  There
have been proposals tha; it is not appropriate for UAA's to be so narrowly
focussed on agriculture and that they should take on additional responsibilities
such as health and family planning as well.  This is a lot to ask of a man who
is poorly trained, is one of the lowest paid government officers, has no housing
(which in the traditional muslim rural setting seriously limits his ability to
visit villages), and is offered virtually no hope of promotion. Small wonder
that farmers report seeing their UAA less than twice a year.  At the Thana level
there is a wide variety of agricultural personnel,2/ but their actual training is
not much better than the UAAs' and their ability to reach farmers is probably
less. Fortunately, farmers seem to be able to adopt major agricultural improvements
without much help from the extension service. But, as refinements in agricultural
technologr become more complex, farmers will need greater technical assis tance.
15.       Farmer training at Thana headquarters in Thana Training and Development
Centers (TTDC) is another method used to convey technical information to farmers.
First developed in Comilla district, it was shown there to have considerable
potential.  Under this system, selected model farmers cone to Thana headquarrers
1/  There are other extension-type activities carrLed out under a number of
area specific and/or crop specific programs.  They have many of the same
problems as the UAA's, plus some of their own.
2/  Including the Thana Agricultural Officer, the Thana Cooperative Officer, the
IRDP Project Officer, the BADC Irrigation Officer, the Plant Protection Officer,
and many more.



-ASICX 3-3
Page 7
periodically to be instructed in new techniques. They then are expected to pass
this information on to others ir. their communities. Unfortunately, overly rapid
expansion combined with the disruptive events of the past several years have
caused serious deterioration in this program. New teaching materials have not
been developed. There is littlE control over the content of the courses as they
are often put together on an ad hoc basis depending on which Thana agricultural
officer happens to be available. The TTDC buildings are sometimes diverted to
other uses.  Small wonder farmers now appear more interested in collecting their
travel allowances than in hearing the same outdated talks time and again. An
FAO/IBRD cooperative program mission began in April 1974L to prepare a rural
development education and training project designed to strengthen the programs
for bringing information to the farmers. Appraisal of this project took
place in July 19741, and work is underway on refining the project.
16.       An effective system of agricultural credit, capable of providing
farmers with timely finance at reasonable interest rates, could ease the
financial burden for farmers of increasing the intensity of fertilizer use.
Unfortunately, institutional (i.a., government/cooperative) agricultural credit
in Bangladesh suffers from serious limitations at the present time.  Agricul-
tural credit in Bangladesh has been the subject of a special sector mission
whose report bas been used to inLtiate a dialogue between the Government
and the Association on ways and mueans of improving agricultural credit.
A brief review of the agricultural credit situation indicates: (a) the
present agricultural credit institutions are, for the most part, in severe
financial trouble;- recovery rates (which are very low) reveal only part
of the problem, for since indepenidence, nearly half of short-term debt sanc-
tioned has been converted by the Bangladesh Bank to medium-term debt, so
the Government would not have to bear the financial burden of carrying out
its obligation to guarantee the 'Loss; (b) at the same time no parallel
debt rescheduling is being carried out at the farm level and recovery of
old debt is not improving; (c) no provision is made in the revenue budget
to guarantee the large volume of lending proposed under the Plan: (d) the
interest rates on agricultural loans are artificially low and do not reflect
the cost of capital, including the administrative costs of the credit system,
and-an allowance for the risks inL lending to farmers; (e) the credit
institutions have not played a significant role in mobilizing rural savings
and resources; and (f) while marx of these problems have been intensified
by the severe hardships of the past three years (and by doubt in farmer's
minds as to their liability for debts incurred before independence), the
problems will remain for several years to come.
17.      The rapid expansion of credit called for in the First Five-Year Plan
would seem inappropriate in the current situation. For reasons of both
finance and implementation capacity, a slower, more careful pace of credit
expansion is indicated. While viable credit institutions may make it easier
to modernize agriculture, the inability of agricultural credit institutions
to expand at the pace proposed in the Plan may not be a serious impediment
to agricultural development in Bangladesh. Comprehensive, recent nation-
wide data on agricultural credit and rural debt do not exist, but some recent
fragmentary data, summarized in Table 2 at the end of this annex, suggest
some interesting points. First, institutional credit provides only a small
portion of the average farmer's btrrowings. Several studies have indicated
that farmers in Bangladesh obtain only about 10 - 15 percent of their bor-
rowings from institutional (goverwmental/cooperative) sources.



AiN: 3-3
Page 8
The largest single source of credit are relatives and friends (52 percent).
Money lenders provide some 30 percent of the credit. Second, less than 5
percent of a farmer's short-term borrowings is used to puirchase agricultural
inputs. The rest is used for consumption and other purposes. The fands
provided by agricultural credit institutions are often not used for agriculture.
Third, only a small part (about 14 percent) of fertilizer purchases are financed
by credit.  All this suggest a fairly weak link between t e present institutional
credit system and the needs of agricultural developmentJ
18.       In spite of grave weaknesses in agricultural credit institutions,
Bangladesh farmers have rapidly increased their use of both fertilizer and HYV
seed. These inputs have apparently been financed largely out of the farmer's
own resources, from sale of small portiors of his crop or from income earned
in subsidiary occupations.  This suggests that at least the initia:! pn-seLi
adoption of the seed-fertilizer technology can begin even though credit
institutions are very weak. One would expect that a more effective rural
credit system might be of special benefit to the small farmholders with
less cash resources who night need to borrow funds just to initially use
high-yielding seeds and fertilizer; but there is no solid evidence that
small farmers hate lagged in the adoption of new technology.2/  Moreover,
the mere expansion of credit flows through existing institutions, which tend
to be dominated by the larger farmers, is not likely to have the desired
effect. This is not to say that an effective credit system would not be a
major asset to agricultural progress, but it does say that mere expansion of
the present system may not yield much return. The implication is that what is
called for is a much slower rate of expansion combined with greater efforts
to improve the quality of the existing institutions.
19.       The limitDpions of the present extension services and credit LnstiLutlons
are a source of serious concern in Bangladesh and various proposals a.,? nder conl-
sideration for strengthening them. It is not likely, however, that the 'eaknesses
of these organizations will prove to be a serious constraint in using the output
of the proposed urea factory and the demand projections in Annex 3-2 reflect fullylr
the known limitations of these services.  Current fertilizer use 4s no- so  'ow
that farmers are a long way from optimum levels.  Firmer-to-farmer troisfers o
information and HYV seeds have done much to spread technological improvrec---t.s 4n
spite of the limitations of the extension service. In the absence of eff. .,'se
institutional credit, traditional sources of finances such as friends,
and money lenders are likely to continue to play a significant role in  ciuw.n
those farmers requiring credit to purchase fertilizers.

BANGLADESH: ASHUGANJ FERTILIZER PROJFET
PRODUCTIDN PROCESS, SUPPLY OF NATURAL GAS AND PLANT FACILITIES
A. Production Process and Supply of Natural Gas
1.        The rated output of -;he plant will be 1,600 TPD urea for 330 days
per annum giving a nominal outl)ut of 528,000 TPY. Actual output if a 90% on-
stream efficiency is maintaine(d will be 475,200 TPY. The intermediate
ammonia production facilities iuill be sized on the basis that all ammonia
produced will be converted to tirec in which case rated output would be
approximately 925 TPD or just rmarginally over 1,000 short tons per day.
2.        Gas from the Titas fields  will be  used for  both process and
fuel and will be supplied to the plant at a pressure of around 600 psig as
required for direct steam reforming without further pressure boosting. It
will be the responsibility of the Titas Gas Transmission Company to supply
and install the necessary gas pipeline up to the works from the existing
high pressure transmission line which passes under the railway bridge across the
Meghna River approximately   2   km north of the plant site.  This line will
run below ground along the route of the access road to the site and the
Titas Gas Transmission Company will provide a suitable metering station
adjacent to the works area and a block valve in the main transmission line
to enable full supplies to be maintained to the fertilizer works indepen-
dently of any downstream failure.
Composition of the ga~3 will be as below:
Methane              96.8%
Ethane                1.7%
Propane               0.4%
Butane and Higher     0.5%
Nitrogen              0.3%
Carbon Dioxide        0.3%
Cal. Value (Gross ) 1,036 BTU/cu.ft.
The gas is classified as sweet vrith respect to sulphur content and the
experience of the Ghorasal workE which is also supplied with the same gas
indicates that sulphur content is below 2 ppm. Producers of the gas, the
Bangladesh Shell Oil Company Lindted, remove liquified hydrocarbons in the
field  so  that    the pipeline gas contains less than 2 US gal./l,000 cu.
ft. The Bangladesh Shell Oil Company Ltd., which is 25% owned by the
Government, operates the field and has determined the total reserves at
about 63 billion cubic meters (M3). Details of the gas reserves for the
major  field in  Bangladesh  including Titas  and gas  analysis  are given
in Annex 4-5. The gas offtake projections for the Titas field are
shown in Annex 4-6. Even assuming the gas demand would continue to
increase at about 11% per year predicted for the remainder of the decade,
the Titas gas reserves would last for another 30 years.



ATNEX 4.1
3.        Synthesis gas for ammonia production is produced from the feed
gas in a series of steps beginning with a primary reforming with steam.
Here, desulphurized gas is combined with superheated steam in an amount
approximately equal to a 3 to 1 steam/carbon ratio. The combined stream
at a pressure of approximately 500-550 psig is then preheated to about 950
degrees F. and passed through catalyst-filled 25/20 chrome nickel alloy
tubes suspended in the radiant section of the reformer. In passing through
the tubes the hydrocarbon components, mostly methane, are partly converted
to carbon oxides and hydrogen and exit at a temperature normally in excess
of 1,500 degrees F.  The partially reformed gas then flows to a secondary
reformer where it is mixed with preheated air in a quantity necessary to
reform the unconverted hydrocarbons and at the same time provide the required
amount of nitrogen for ammonia synthesis. Air required for the secondary
reforming step. is supplied from a centrifugal steam turbine driven compressor.
4.        Reformed gas from the secondary reformer now containing less than
about 0.4% methane is cooled in waste heat boilers down to about 700 degrees F.
prior to introduction to the high temperature shift converter where part of
the carbon monoxide content of the gas is converted to carbon dioxide by
reaction with steam with an equivalent production of hydrogen. To complete
this conversion of carbon monoxide the gas after cooling in additional waste
heat boilers and after a protective fine sulphur removal is introduced into
a second stage low temperature converter where the carbon monoxide content
of the gas is reduced to about 0.5%.
5.        The raw synthesis gas now contains essentially hydrogen, nitrogen,
carbon dioxide and small amounts of hydrocarbons and carbon monoxide and now
goes through a series of purification steps to provide an ammonia synthesis
gas with a hydrogen/nitrogen ratio of 3 to 1.  In the first of these carbon
dioxide is removed by any of a number of proprietary liquid scrubbing processes
and then remaining carbon oxides are removed by conversion to methane by
reaction with hydrogen over a suitable catalyst. The final synthesis gas
then contains in addition to nitrogen and hydrogen, less than about 10 ppm
oxides, the maximum which can be tolerated by the ammonia synthesis catalyst,
and small amounts of methane which are continually purged from the following
ammonia synthesis loop.
6.        The carbon dioxide removed from the crude synthesis gas is
regenerated from the rich liquid absorbent and is subsequently reacted with
ammonia to form urea. The analysis of the natural gas from the Titas Gas
fields, however, is such that the quantity of carbon dioxide produced in the
gas reforming process described will not be sufficient for urea production
and must be supplemented. The additional quantity required may be obtained
either by absorption from reformer or auxiliary boiler flue gas followed by
regeneration from the absorbent, or by complete combustion of a side stream
of natural gas followed by absorption from the combustion products, or in
exceptional cases by the excess production of crude synthesis gas with
recycling of the excess quantity after carbon dioxide removal, back to the
primary reformer where it is burned as fuel.



AVNisX h-i
Page 3
7.        In the synthesis of ammonia the nitrogen v-rdrogen mixtLure is
compressed to a pressure of 2,500-3,000 psi by a           -te,; turbine driven 2 or
3 case centrifugal compressor with recycle gas contaiLnng about 11% ammonia
being admitted to an interstage wheel. The coompressed discharge gas after
water cooling is then refrigerated and condensed amon.-L,i res-oved in a liquid
separator before the gases now Containing only abort 2% ar.nrnia are reheated
by heat exchange and passed to trhe ammonia Converter. This vessel is of a
special design containing a catalyst to promr&te the syTnthesis reaction and
special provision for removal of the reaction heat developed, so as to main-
tain optimum reaction temperatures.  The effluent gas from the Converter
contains about 11% NH3 and is coJoled from the rea.ction temperature of about
9o0 degrees F. first in preheating boiler feed water and then by heat
exchange with incoming converter: feed gas, before recycling to the synthesis
gas compressor as described.
3.        Liquid ammonia producod in the process wJll be stored in a
refrigerated liquid ammonia storage tank having a capacity of about 5-6 days
production or some 5,000 tons.
9.        In the production of t!rea, ammonia and carbon dioxide are combined
at a pressure of 2,000-3,500 ps, and a temperature of approximately 360
degrees F. when ammonium carbama.te is formed as an intermediate product,
which is subsequently dehydratec with formation of uirea.  Neither of these
reactions, however, proceeds to completion with the result that the reaction
product consists of a urea/ammorium carbamate solut-ion containing large
quantiities of dissolved amnmnia and carbon dioxide.  The next step in the
production of urea therefore is to separate the non-converted carbamate and
reaction gases and recycle them to the reactor, and the means of doing this
represents the major distinction between the various processes commercially
available.  In one commonly used process t1he pressure of the reaction products'
is reduced in steps with consequent decomposition of carbamate back into
carbon dio.xide and ammonia which are then redissolved by countercurrent
absorption and recycled back to bhe reactor.  In another process the decom-
position is achieved without reduction in pressuare by stripping the solution
leavi-ng the r eactorwith carbon lioxide and in another process a similar
stripping pre.^ess is used employi ng gaseous ammonia. In all these processes
the final urea solutio  is concentrated by evaporation and then in most
cases prilled d1rectly if a biuret concentration of up to 1-1/h percent can
be tolerated, o.; after vacuum crrstalization if a low biuretj grade is
require;.  For thfe project which will produce urea with up to 0.9% biuret,
a preselect.ion of -urea process is not being made and instead a selection will
be made based on the merits of tie proposal.s reeived fr om contractors both
at the prequalification and bidd`ng stage.
1/ Less than 0.5%.

B. Plant Facilities
10.       Of the approximate total area of 500 acres already acquired at
Ashuganj by the EFCPC, some 130 acres initially will be filled to a level
of 32 feet to accommodate the proposed plant, the staff housing complex,
and necessary access roads and rail sidings. It is estimated that the level
of this area will have to be raised by an average of 20 feet requiring the
dredging of approximately 4-1/2 million cubic yards of sand from the bed of
the Meghna River. Preliminary estimates indicate that the plant site will
oocupy approximately 65 acres, the housing complex 45 acres, with the balance
being taken up by rail sidings, access roads and vacant areas. It is planned
that construction of an embankment around the perimeter of the area to be
developed and main access road connecting the site to the Ashuganj railway
bridge area would be completed in the dry season of 1974/75 permitting the
dredging work to continue through the wet season of 1975 with completion
being scheduled around October.
11.       After reclamation is completed and after allowing a reasonable
period for settlement, civil works on the plant site and construction of the
first stage of the staff housing complex would begin. The staff housing
complex will accommodate all key staff to be employed by the works when
completed, such as management and supervisory personnel, shift operators and
key production and maintenance workers and security and safety personnel.
Currently the total number of personnel estimated to fall into this category
is around 525 and it is planned to lay out the housing complex accordingly.
A later review of requirements, however, will be carried out at the time of
detailed planning of the complex. Initially the houses will be utilized for
accommodating key personnel engaged in the plant construction phase.
12.       A wharf will be constructed at the plant site designed to be
capable of handling the loading of the entire output from the works onto
coasters or suitable barges for local and export delivery as desired. At
this stage the merits of shipment of part of the product in bulk rather than
in bags is being studied and until a final decision is reached the design
of the wharf facilities will not be defined. However, it is intended that
modern materials conveying and loading equipment will be provided. It is
anticipated that a large portion of the plant output will be handled by rail
and for this a railway spur and sidings will be provided connecting the plant
site with the existing nearby Bangladesh Railway system. Necessary freight
cars will be provided by Bangladesh Railways but two locomotives will be
included as part of the project for the movement of freight cars within the
plant area and adjacent sidings.
13.       Water requirements of the plant and housing complex will be taken
from the Meghna River which has a very low salinity at the plant location.
Raw river water will first be clarified in a circular basin with continuous
mechanical sludge removal and then filtered in automatic self-cleaning sand
filters before being supplied to the potable water chlorination and supply
system, to the demineralizers for boiler feed water, to the plant cooling



AN1NEX 4-1
Page 5
water system make up and to other general uses. At this stage it is anti-
cipated that a recirculation cooling water system will be adopted but the
merits of an alternative once-through scheme will be evaluated in the course
of more detailed planning of the works.
14.       Preliminary estimateE indicate that the entire works will consume
approximately 10 MW of electric power. The nearby Ashuganj power station
has adequate spare generating capacity to supply this requirement if desired
but at present pending further examination of the reliability of this source,
provision has been made for caFtive power generation facilities to be
included as part of the project, with the existing power station providing
standby only. Final decision on the power source will be based on maximum
reliability and the economic and technical merits of proposals to be received
from the designers.
15.       All necessary buildings and stores required  for the efficient and
continuous operation of the works will be included in the project. Among
these are a 2,000 sq. meter fully equipped maintenance shop, gate house, fire
station, laboratory, canteen, first aid station and adequate warehouses for
the storage of spare parts and other stores. For the storage of bulk urea a
45-day or 72,000-ton capacity bullk store will be provided fitted out to main-
tain dehumidified air conditionLng necessary for the prevention of caking.
Additionally, a bagged urea store of 15-day or 24,000-ton capacity will be
included.
16.       The type of bag to be used for the urea product is still the subject
of investigation and may be a 2';-kg capacity polyethylene lined jute bag or
a single film heavy duty polyethylene bag.  The plant will be equipped with
facilities for the manufacture either of the polyethylene liners or of the
polyethylene bags as the case may be. A final decision will be taken during
the course of the design of the plant and will be largely influenced by bag
production costs and recommendation of the marketing consultants as to the
preferred type of bag under prevailing conditions of fertilizer distribution
and usage.
17.       Special attention will be paid during design and specifications of
the facilities to minimize the emission and/or discharge of noxious waste
materials. Wherever possible these materials will be recycled within the
process units. Additionally, the design of the process units will be in
accordance with European and U.S. standards with respect to the composition
of liquid and gaseous effluents. Under these conditions and especially with
the added advantage of having a clean sulphur free raw material it is expected
that the works will have little :r no undesirable effect on the enviroment.

BANGLADESH-_ASHUGANJ FERTILIZER PROJECT
ASHUGANJ FERTILIZER AND CHEMICAL COMPANY
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR MANAGEMENT,
OPERATIONAL AND TRAINING ASSISTANCE
1.        The Government of the Peoples Republic of Bangladesh proposes to
erect a modern ammonia/urea fertilizer complex at Ashuganj on the east bank
of the river Meghna some 60 km northeast of Dacca. The purposes of the
project are to increase the country's capacity to produce fertilizer to
meet local demand, and to the extent that local demand is met, to produce
surplus urea for export. The project will consist of a 1,600 ton per day
(TPD) urea unit, an intermediate ammonia unit of approximately 925 TPD, and
all necessary supporting services, utilities and auxiliary plants and
installations for a fully operable manufacturing complex established on a
grass roots site.  A fully self contained staff housing compound to accommo-
date some 600 staff will be included.
2.        The project will use as its raw material, natural gas supplied
to the site from the nearby Titas gasfields by the Titas Gas Transmission and
Distribution Company. The urea will be dispatched from the works in bags
by barge from a jetty adjacent to the plant site to be constructed
as part of the project and by rail via an existing branch line passing
within 2 km of the plant site.  Distribution and marketing of the urea
fertilizer will be handled by, and will be the responsibility of, the
Bangladesh Agricultural Development Corporation, a Government Corporation
forming part of the Department of Agriculture.
3.        To implement and operate the project, the Government has estab-
Ushed an entirely new company "The Ashuganj Fertilizer and Chemical
Company Limited" (The Company), under the Companies Act 1913 with all shares
held by the Government.  The Company was incorporated in October 1974
with a capital of TX 1,000,000,000 and with a board of directors of not
less than three and not more than seven members. For the time being the
board will be made up of two nominees of BFCPC*, two nominees of the
Government, and the Managing Director who shall be the Chief Executive of
the Company.
.*The Bangladesh Fertilizer, Chemical and Pharmaceutical Corporation (BFCPC), is
an autonomous state corporation wholly owned by the Government of Bangladesh
and comprising some fifty individual manufacturing enterprises in the
fertilizer, chemical and pharmaceutical industries.  The BFCPC presently
operates four fertilizer manufacturing works as follows:
1.        A 106,000 TPY urea plant and ammonium sulphate-unit at Fenchlgan;.
2.        A 34.0,000 TPY urea plant at Ghorasal.
3.        A 32,000 TPY triple superphosphate (TSP) plant at Chittagong.
4.        A 120,000 TPY TSP plant at Chittagong.

and purchase land, plant, machinery and eqIuipment for a urea fertilizer

will have the power to delegate authority to anyone for implementing the
policy, and for ensuring the best use of .k,e assets of the Company by
achieving optimum efficiency against budgeted expenditure and to ensure
the development of the Company's industrial and commercial potentialities
to the highest level. The general direction and management of the affairs
of the Company shall rest in the Directors0

4. Entered into a contract with the Vinnel Corporation
(Alhambra-USA) in October 1974 for preparation of the
plant site preparatory to construction of the project.
The scope of work under this contract includes
dredging of materials from the bed of the Meghna River
so as to fill approximately 190 acres of the area of
the project site up to construction level; construction
of marginal embankments as required to hold the dredged
fill; and construction of e2nbankments for an approach
road to the plant site and a connecting road between the
plant site and the housing compound area. These works
are scheduled for completion by November 1975 thus
permitting plant construction work to be commenced
early 1976.
5.       These steps having been taken towards execution of the project, the
Company is now at the stage of calling bids for an Engineering Firm for the
prime design, procurement and construction contract. At the same time, the
Company wishes to appoint a suitably qualified and experienced firm (herein-
after called the Management Assistance Firm) to assist its staff in managing
the Company during the implementation and operation of the project. In this
regard it is envisaged that such assistance would take the form of a team of
experts provided by the Management Assistance Firm to assume key positions in
the senior and middle management organization of the Company with line respon-
sibility through to the Company's Managing Director. In addition to these
line responsibility positions it is also envisaged that the team should include
a limited number of advisors who would assume such advisory roles in relation
to certain key positions to be filled from the outset by Bangladesh nationals.
It is intended therefore that the management of the new Company shall be com-
posed of a blend of Bangladesh nationals and expatriate experts seconded from
the Management Assistance Firm. It is further intended that the senior and
mi'ddle management organization of the Company shall initially be adapted
to the needs of the Company in controlling the physical and financial progress
of construction of the project and shall be reorientated and expanded as
required to achieve a smooth and efficient transformation to the organiz-
ational needs of an ongoing urea manufacturing works as the construction
phase is completed. It is presently planned that participation of the
Management Assistance Firm will commence in the first quarter of 1975 and
continue through 2 to 3 years after plant start-up. During this period
the firm will in addition to its line and advisory responsibilities outlined
above carry out an appropriate training program aimed at ultimate take-over
of all positions within the Company by Bangladesh nationals. This program
will cover ongoing practical training which will be achieved by close
co-operation of expatriate experts and Bangladesh nationals in day-to-day
operations utilizing the relationship of "expatriate advisor" where a key
position is filled from the outset by a Bangladesh national, or of "Bang-
ladesh National understudy" where such a position is initially to be
filled by an expatriate expert and later assumed by a Bangladesh national.
Additionally the Management Assistance Firm will be required to undertake
a formal ongoing training program for staff in addition to the intensive
short duration, plant operation training to be given to key technical and
operational personnel under arrangements to be made with the general
contractor.



ANNEX 4-6
Page h
6.        For the implementation of the project the Company (including the
Management Assistance Firm) wilL be aided by the Technical Advisor who will,
in addition to preparing tender documents, undertake evaluation of bids for
the General Contract and subsequently monitor the detailed technical work
of the contractor, and by the Gemeral Contractor who will assume turnkey
responsibility for the design, procurement and construction of the project
on a cost plus fixed fee basis. The specific roles of the technical advisor
and the general contractor as they bear on their relationship to the Company
(including the Management Assistance Firm) are as described below:
1. General Contractor (Engineering Firm)
The Company proposes to enter into a contract (or contracts) with
a single firm for all project management, engineering, purchasing, construction
supervision, start-up supervision and other such services as necessary
to provide it with a complete operating facility. It is anticipated that
the contract with such firm will be awarded on a lunp sum basis for all
services, with equipment and materials, etc., to be reimbursable at cost.
While the Company will enter into a contract or contracts with only one
firm, such firm may associate ito3elf with one or more other prequalified
firms to undertake the necessary total responsibility. The general contract
as entered into between the Compaxy and the General Contractor will be subject
to the approval of the Lenders and any equipment, materials, supplies or
services procured by the General Contractor for the project on behalf of
the Company to be financed by the Lenders shall be subject to their approval.
The General Contractor shall be fully responsible for the completion of the
project according to an agreed t metable and for arranging intensive short
duration operational training to be given to the Company's operators in an
existing works operating similar processes and of similar complexity.  It
is anticipated that tenders for the General Contract will be called in
November 1974 and an award made Iby April 1, 1975.
2. Technical Advisor
The existing contract dated February 12,1974 between the Technical
Advisor (joint venture between Scientific Design Company Ltd. and James
Chemical Engineering) and the BFCPC will be taken over by the Company.
Duties of the Technical Advisor included in the contract are summarized as
follows:
(a)  Prepare invitation3 to bid for the General Contract for the
design, procurement, construction and start-up of
the complete project on a turnkey basis.
(b)  Analyze applications for prequalification to bid the
General Contract a; received by the Company and propose
a list of bidders for such contract to the Company.
(c) Analyze and evaluate all bids received for the General
Contract and make recommendationsto the Company for
award.
(d) Assist the Company- in negotiating a satisfactory agreement
with the chosen General Contractor.



ANNEX 4-6
Page 5
(e)  Assist the Company in making submissions to the Lenders.
(f) Review and monitor the General Contractor's process
and Mechanical design, equipment specifications,
cost estimates and progress schedules.
(g) Review with the Company and give technical and commercial
recommendations in respect to the General Contractor's
analysis of bids and recommended awards of purchase
contracts to be made by the Company, and submit copies to
the Lenders where required.
(h) On behalf of the Company review and monitor the operations
of the General Contractor and his subcontractors in
respect to design, procurement, expediting, inspection,
logistics and construction to ensure that the facilities
are being constructed in workmanlike manner and in
conformity with timetables, budgets and specifications.
(i)  Provide the Company with monthly report on progress of
the project.
(j) During final phases of construction, provide super-
vision of the General Contractor and subcontractor's
work in testing and preparing the facilities for initial
operation.
(k) Assist the Company in supervision of the General Contractor
to ensure a full set of records is compiled for the plant
and equipment as built.
(1) Provide the Company with any and all other technical
assistance it may require from time to time.
(m)  Assist the Company to arrange to have Company staff receive
training in a fertilizer plant of similar type of plant
being constructed by the General Contractor.
(n)  Assist the Company in conducting test runs in conformity
with contractural obligations of the several suppliers
and contractors. Analyze test runs and report.
(o) Assist the Company in checking design drawings, operating
and maintenance manuals, spare parts lists and other
such documents.
7.       In the context of the above roles assigned to the General Contractor
and Technical Advisor, and their relationships to the Company, the specific
role and responsibilities of the Management Assistance Firm as assumed by the
Individuals in its team working as part of the Company's organization are
described firstly by reference to the tentative organization charts shown



ANNEX 4-6
Tage 6
in Charts I and II attached hereto. The general form of the organization
which may be adopted by the Company entering the operating phase is as
shown in Chart I down to middls management level. This organization as
shown is divided into two grou:s (factory and finance) coming under a
Managing Director/General Manag er (directly responsible to the Board) who
would be a Bangladesh national having the assistance of an expatriate advisor.
Additionally a Training Manager (expatriate expert) would be directly
responsible to the General Manager.  The organization chart of Chart I
is indicative only at this stage, and is subject to later revisions. The
purpose of its inclusion in the;se Terms of Reference is to indicate the
scope of responsibilities required from the Management Assistance Firm
and not to define the organization which may be later adopted by the
Company. Management of the wcr ks would fall into three groups, namely
production, maintenance, and technical, each of which would be headed by
an expatriate manager reporting to the Factory Manager who would be a
Bangladesh national. Financia2 management of the Company would be init-
ially entrusted to an expatriate who would be assisted by a Chief Account-
ant, an Administrative Manager and Commercial Manager the latter two of
whom are expected to be expatriates. Under this basic framework the chart
shows 37 key positions 21 of which would initially be filled by experts
from the Management Assistance Firm either in a line position or in an
advisory role.  Chart II shows this organization developing through the
various phases of the project commencing from the stage when bids are called
for the General Contract, progressing through the design and construction
periods and leading into the operational phase. The chart shows the key
executive strength of the Company increasing from seven at the time of
award of the general contract to 13-24 during the construction phase and
finally to 37 at the commencement of operations. Chart II also shows the
transition of the positions and responsibilities prevailing during the
implementation phase to those irn the manufacturing phase and categorizes
each position as:
B   -  Position to be filled by Bangladesh national.
E/B - Position to be filled by expatriate expert with
Bengalee understudy.
B/E - Position to be filled by Bangladesh national with
expatriate advisor.
The overall schedule shown in Chart II covers up to 4 years for implement-
ation followed by 3 years operation, at the end of which the participation
of the Management Assistance Firm would be expected to be phased out and
all its experts replaced by BangLadesh nationals.

2. Operational Phase
As the construction of the project nears completion the Company
organization which will have hitlherto centered around a "project team'" will
be transf6rmed so as to take on -;he normal form of an established
urea fertilizer manufacturing coripany.  This transformation is indicated
in Chart TT where the individuals holding key positions in the project teamr.
will assume new roles and resporusibilities in the manufacturing organization.
It should be noted, however, that. the organization of the project team, the
organization of the manufacturing: company and the transformation of one into
the other as illustrated in Charts I and IIare set forth as a guide only,
and variations or alternatives may be proposed. The responsibilities of
the Companyts manufacturing orgarization which shall be shared by the
Management Assistance Firm's representatives will, however, be inter alia
as follows:
(a) To operate the manifacturing facilities to the
maxinmn attainable level of output consistent with
safe and efficient operational practices.
(b) To maintain a high level of quality control, and to
this end develop and implement an appropriate system
of quality control, testing and recording procedures.
(e)  To maintain the Comipany's plant and equipment in top
condition and sounc. working order so that a continuing
high on-stream utilization can be anticipated; with
this objective establish an efficient system of
preventive and rc.utine maintenance based on adequate
equipment records and operating histories. * aintain
and consolidate the spare parts inventory and control
system instituted during construction of the project.
(d) Establish and maintain a suitable stores control
system covering all raw materials and consumables
utilized in the CoPrany's operations and all products
dispatched and in sJorage.
(e) Develop, implement .md administer an appropriate
personnel policy to serve the best interests of the
Company and its sta'f.
(f) Develop and adopt appropriate manufacturing standards
with respect to safety, operational and maintenance
practices, environMEntal and pollution control, etc.



ANNEX 4-6
Page 11
(g) Develop and implement appropriate production control
procedures including accurate measurements and main-
tenance of records of consumption of raw materials,
utilities and services and other inputs, compared to
productions of intermediates, by products and final
products, so as to maintain a high production efficiency
and to minimize losses and identify sources of plant
operational inefficiencies.
(h) Institute and operate an appropriate training program
aimed at developing and maintaining a high level
of proficiency amongst operational, technical,
maintenance, financial and administrative staff.
(i) Institute a security system aimed at protecting the
Company's assets and develop disaster control procedures
with adequate staff instruction and training.
(j) Take out adequate insurance coverage for all the
Company's assets and against Workmen's Compensation
and third party claims.
(k) Develop and implement an appropriate cost control
system covering costs of all inputs, intermediates
and final products.
(1) Prepare production level and manufacturing cost
budgets. Implement systems whereby performance against
these budgets is regularly checked and results reported
to management.
(m) Develop and implement a modern and effective system
of accounts to be adopted by the Company in respect
of all its commercial transaction and internal transfers.
(n) Develop and implement reporting systems to be adopted
at all levels of the Comoany's operations.
(o) Prepare company manuals covering operational, safety,
accounting, budgeting and reporting procedures.
(p) Develop and implement whatever other management and
control techniques may be necessary to ensure the
Company is able to operate efficiently and profitably
on a continuing basis.
(q) Maintain adequate financial and accounting records
covering all the Company's assets and its operations.
(r) Prepare quarterly financial statements (balance sheets,
statements of income and expenses, etc.) for each
quarter of the Company's fiscal year and furnish such
statements to the Lenders within 30 days after end
of each such quarter.

ANNEX 5-6
Page 1
BANGLADESH: ASHUGANJ FERTILIZER PROJECT
ALLOCATION OF PROCUREMENT
1.        The financing package covering the foreign exchange costs of
the project (para. 5.04) consistsof some tied and some untied funds as
follows:
In Millions of US Dollar Equivalent
Tied Funds          Untied `unds
IDA                                    33.0
ADB_-/                                 30.0
AIDi/              30
ODI42/             18
GOI                                    12.4
KFW                                    12.0
GOS                                     6.5
Total            4                   93.9
1/ Substantially untied--available for procurement in nearly all ADB member
countries.
2/ Available for procurement in USA and developing countries only.
5/ Only available for procuremflent in the UK.
In order to achieve most efficient use of these available funds
with resultant minimum project costs it becomes necessaxy to plan procurement
so that maximum flexibility in choice of supply sources is retained until
all necessary expenditures have been committed. In this way the situation
is avoided whereby towards the end of procurement it is found that only tied
funds remain whereas the goods still to the procured cannot be obtained at
competitive prices from the remaining supply source or in an extreme case
may not be available from that source at all. In the case of this project
it has been adjudged as by no means certain that the entire tied component
of the full financing package can be utilized in the normal course of inter-
national bidding, in which case some of these funds would remain after all
untied funds had been consumed. This being so it has been considered prudent
to hasten the use of the untied funds by preallocation of certain goods
and/or services to each tied fund source, ensuring at the same time that the
goods and/or services so preallocated can be competitively supplied from
the respective source countries.
2.        While such preallocation to the tied fund supply sources has been
necessary for most efficient use of the available finances as a whole, some
preallocation has also been adopted in the case of the untied fund sources
to reduce the administrative burden of administering a mixed pool of funds
on so complex a project. Thus it has been seen to be desirable to preallocate
the whole of the ammonia plant equipment on a f.o.b. basis to the ADB and the
whole of the urea plant equipment on a similar basis to the KfW. These two
segments of the project may, therefore, be separated from a procurement
administration aspect from the remainder of Uhe project and supervised by
the ADB and KfT,I respectively, thus relieving the other lenders of a con-
siderable area of procurement responsibility.



ANNEX 5-6
Page 2
3. Preallocation of procuremelnt for the project may be summarized as follows:
(a) Asian Developmenit Bank (Untied funds)
All battery limits ammonia plant equipment on ail (f.o.b.)
basis amounting to $30 million.
(b) KfW (Untied funds)
All battery lim:its Urea plant equipment on an (f.o.b.) basis
up to a value oi' $12 million.
(c)  US AID (Tied fuwds)
A package of equipment to the value of $12.6 million plus the
site dredging anud filling contract estimated to cost.$8.1
million making a total of $20.7 million. An amount of $9.3
million, therefore, remains which is anticipated tQ be won by
US companies after international competitive bidding.
(d) ODM (Tied funds)
A package of equipment amounting to $11.7 million leaving a
balance of $7.3 million which    is anticipated to be won
by UK companies after international bidding.  In the event,
however, that a UK firm wins the General Contract (Engineering
Firm), then thi.; preallocated equipment package will be with-
drawn and instead a portion of the value of the General Contract
will be allocated for UK financing with the balance to be financed
by IDA.
4.        Details of each of th.e above described preallocated procurement
packages are set out below:

BANGLADESH: ASHJIANJ FERTIIZER PROJECT
LICENSES, DUTIES, 1NIAND TRANSPORT AND CONTRACT APPROVAL
A. Import Licenses and Customs Duties
1.        The Govarnment will provride without delay all import licenses
for the importation of Project goods procured from foreign sources.
2.        Customs duties, at the rates in force from time to time (presently
from 25% to 60%), will be charged on the goods imported for the purposes of
the Project. To facilitate entry of the goods into Bangladesh and delivery
to site, the Government will implement the following procedure: Customs
duty will be assessed and become payable immediately upon the goods arrival,
or in the case of shipment by sea., upon presentation of shipping documents
(prior to the ship's arrival) to {;he appropriate Customs Authorities. Payment
of the duty will be facilitated by the establishment of a deposit or imprest
account for this purpose or such cother means acceptable to IDA. After payment
of duty and upon arrival of the goods by sea,, the goods will be unloaded to
cargo barges for transshipment to the site. Upon arrival of goods by air
and payment of duties against shipping documents, the goods will be promptly
transshipped to the plant site. IFpon arrival at site, the goods will be
unloaded to shore where they will be promptly inspected by Customs appraisers
(located at site at the expense of the Company) to determine that the goods
delivered to site correspond to the description of the goods in the shipping
documents against which Customs duty had been paid. Any adjustments in
Customs duty will be effected in due course, but the goods, after Customs
appraisal will be released from Customs control immediately and may be
utilized in the manner required for the Project.
B. Inland Water Transportation of Proj ect Goods
3.        To facilitate delivery of foreign Project goods to the site, they
will be promptly transshipped at the ports of entry from ocean-going vessels
to inland water cargo barges for direct shipment to the site. Upon adequate
notice to the Government of the type and quantity of Project goods required
to be shipped through inland water to the site, the Government will ensure the
timely availability, through its appropriate agencies, of cargo barges suitable
for such purpose. Should such cargo barges not be available, the Company
may take such steps as it consider3 fit to arrange for such shipment and the
Government will give all necessary assistance in this respect.
C. Procurement of Imported SDare Parts and Imported Consumable Stores
Required for the Operation of the Plantt
14.       In procuring the above-mentioned items, the Company may, at its
discretion, utilize the services oE BFCPC. The Government will ensure,
throughout the life of the Project, the availability of   sufficient foreign
exchange resources to permit the Company to make timely purchases of such
spare parts and stores as are requLred for the efficient functioning of the
Project, and will ensure the issuaice of such import licenses and provision
of such assistance as will enable -he spare parts and stores to be imported
into Bangladesh and delivered expeditiously to the site.



ANNEX 5-7
Page 2
D. Goverrment Approval of Contracts Exceeding Tk 10 Lakhs (US$0.13 million)
5.        The following procedures and rules shall apply'to the proposed
award of contracts by the Company and to all contracts to which the Company
is, or proposes to become, party for the purchase of goods and services in
connection with the execution of the Project. Such proposed awards and
contracts, the expenditures in respect of which are not estimated to exceed
taka ten lakhs (US$0.13 million) equivalent, shaU not be subject to any
domestic review or approval whatsoever, except by the Company, To the extent
the approval of any authority of the Government (the "Approving Authority")
may be required by law, the following procedures shall apply:
(a) If the Approving Authority approves the proposed award
of a contract, and such contract as agreed between the
Company and the successful bidder does not differ
substantially from that anticipated by the terms of
the proposed award and does not significantly alter
the obligations of the Company beyond those customarily
undertaken in connection with similar contracts (except
to the extent anticipated by such terms), then the Company
will not be required to submit for further review, or
obtain any further approval of, such contract and may
execute and deliver the same with legally binding effect.
(b) The Government shall convey its decision in respect of
any proposed contract award to the Company within
fourteen days of the date of receipt of the bid
evaluations, including the proposed award, from the
General Contractor by the Company's Dacca office.
Such award shall be assumed to have been approved,
and the Company (or if the Company so decides, by the
General Contractor for the benefit of the Company) may
proceed, upon compliance with any applicable requirement
of the schedule to the Project Agreement, to execute and
deliver the proposed oontract with legally binding effect,
if the Borrower's decision in respect of the proposed
contract award is not communicated to the Company within
21 days of such date.

BANGLADESH - ASHUGANJI FERTILIZER PROJECT
STAFFING, SALARY AND WAGE SCHEDULE
The factory wages and administrative salaries for the proposed ferti-
lizer plant are based on the recent (1973) recommendations of two commissions:
the national wage (factory) commission and the national salary (officers) com-
mission for public enterprises and government agencies.
The wage commission recommended that starting 1974/75, the starting
basic monthly wage rate for unskilled, semi-skilled and skilled workers be raised
by an average of 20%, 27% and 30% respectively, from the present levels in the
public chemical industries.  Annual wage increases of 3 to 5% are also recom-
mended. The maximum basic wage for each of the 3 classes of labor above are
roughly 150%, 165% and 175% of the starting rates, respectively.
In contrast to the large wage increases recommended for the factory
workers by the wage commission, the national commission on public salaries
recommended that the top management, officers, and professional personnel have
a maximum basic salary of Tk 2,000 per month. This -would mean a cut in the
salary of top level officers who are presently earning from Tk 2,500 to Tk 3,500
per month.
On the average, the middle mmnagement salary levels remain unchanged.
There are only slight reductions in the upper-middle levels and slight increases
in the lower-middle levels.
The reconmended salary levels for lower management, clerical and simrilar
employees are 15 to 25, higher than existing levels.  Annual salary increases of
2 to 7%, are also recommended.
In addition to the basic salaries and wages, fringe benefits such as
housing, transportation, medical and children's educational allowances, leave and
holiday pay, and retirement benefits are to be continued, but at slightly higher
rates than are presently provided.  Fcr manufacturing enterprises, the production
bonus system is also to be continued.
The operating staff of the fertilizer factory will fall under the
:ollowing salary or wage levels:

i/ The minimum and maximum salary or wage in each range. Strictly speaking,
there is a normal range and an extended range for each level. The maximum
shown above is for the extended range. The normal range provides for about
5 to 10 years of annual increases.
b/ Factory workers, foremen and technicians have a recommended range of 155 to 850.
Lower level administrative and clerical type personnel have a recommended range
of 130 - 975. Several grades of workers such as unskilled, semi-skilled and
skilled as well as 5 grades of employee salary levels have been combined.
The basic annual salary and wages for the proposed fertilizer plant are
roughly Tk 6.10 million. This is calculated based on a more detailed breakdown
of employees for each of 10 salary grades for administrative staff and each of 6
Wage grades for factory workers.  T'he mid-point of the normal range for each
salary or wage grade was used, representing roughly 5 years of annual increases
flrom the minimum salary or wage. This factor is judged to be sufficient to cover
any wage or salary escalation from 1974 to 1978 and allow for a mix of varying
experienced personnel in the initial staff.
Fringe benefits, as recormended in the commission reports, vary
according to location and grade level. Nevertheless, the recommended total
fringe benefit package is less than that experienced at the Ghorasal Fertilizer
factory. At this factory, fringe benefits for the entire staff comes to about
50% of the basic salaries and wages. This proportion is used in this report.
Fertilizer plants must provide incentives to attract qualified people to remote
places where the factory is located.

1/ The BFCPC overhead charges are based on the current allocation procedure.
Each enterprise is charged a proportion of the BFCPC total charges equal
to the ratio of the installed cost of the fixed assets of the enterprise
to the installed cost of the f-xed assets of all the EFCPC enterprises.



ANNEX 6-3
The installed or original cost of the fixed assets of all the existing
(1974) BFCPC enterprises is presently Tk 762 million (US$101.6 million).
The proposed fertilizer plant will have fixed assets installed at $216.9
Million.  (Capital cost $249.4 million less management assistance
including contingency of $7.9 million and the net working capital of
$20.8 million including $8.0 million for spares and 2 sets of catalysts
included in equipment cost estimates.) The local currency equivalent is
thus Tk 1,654 million.  Consequently, the proposed fertilizer plant will
bear about 68% of the total BFCPC overhead charges. The present (1972/73)
BFCPC overhead charges total Tk 6.0 million (although expenditures come
to only Tk 4.0 million).  This Tk 6.0 million is escalated by 50% to
TTk 9.0 million, and used as the base for the BFCPC overhead charges
against the proposed plant.

such a catalysts, chemicals, P.E. chips and maintenance materials are
valued at their cif cost plus local handling at domestic market prices.
The shadow price of loca-1 labor, services, and goods are taken as equal
to the market prices (less excise taxes where appropriate). These are
converted to US dollars using the official exchange rate of Tk 7.50 per
US dollar. The economic price of natural gas is assumed to be identical
to the financial price of Tk 3.72 (US$050) per MSCF.

jute bags was Tk 2.80 per bag at the end of 1973. This is only Tk 0.30
per bag more than the delivered price to the local factory.  There has
been no study on the relative econcmnic advantage of either jute versus
heavy P.E. bags. This.will be one of the subjects to be investigated by
the fertilizer nar!tthn*'4istribution study.

ANNEX 6-7

BANGIADESH-ASHUGA.NJ FERTILIZER PROJECT
FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
A. Assumptions for Financial Projections
1.         The project is assumed to start comrercial operations August, 1978,
and a forecast period of twelve years to June 1990 is used. The factory is
depreciated over twelve years on a straight line basis. Capacity utilization
is estimated at 50% during the five months of 1978, 75% during the following
year and 90% thereafter. The production costs are as shown in Annex 6-3 and
represent estimated mid-1978 costs which, with the exception of
natural gas, are about 40-50% higher than prices prevailing in mid-1974. The
cost escalation is based on the assumed annual inflation rates shown in Annex
6-8. The long term debt of 142.3 million is onlent by the Government to AFCC
at 10% annual interest for fifteen years (starting December 1974) including a
five-year grace period. The debt is repaid in equal, semi-annual payments of
$7.11 million starting January 1, 1980, the start of the third operating year.
The final payment is made July of 1989. Interest is also paid semi-annually
on the outstanding balance, with the first payment on January 1, 1979, tg &over
interest charges for the three month period October to December of 1978.1/'
The corporate income tax rate is 55%, but operating losses can be carried forward
to offset taxable inceme during subsequent years.
2.         The cost structure of the natural gas is described in paras. 4 and
5 of Annex 7-1. A price of Tk 3.72 ($0.50) per MSCF is assumed in this
analysis. This price is about 60% higher than the late 1974 price and was set
by the Government as a basis for the projections and the gas purchase contract
between AFCO and the gas transmission coipany.
B. Urea Fertilizer Prices
3.         As of the middle of March 1074, urea fertilizer was priced as
follows:
Retail price    : $107 per metric ton.
Ex-Factory price: $103 per metric ton.
The distribution cost was subsidized by the Government, hence the spread
between the retail price and the ex-factory price was small. Towards the
end of March 1974, however, following the Government policy of a phased
reduction in subsidies (see Section B, Annex 3-3 for details), retail prices
were raised by almost 67% to $179 per metric ton to eliminate the Government
subsidy on urea and also to subsidize the sales of other types of fertilizers,
which are still retailed at prices below cost. The ex-factory price for the
existing plants have not been officially increased, but BFCPC has initiated
action in this direction.
1/ The interest during construction was calculated up to September 1978.



INNEX 6-7
Page 2
4.         During appraisal, the Government has agreed that the ex-factory
price will be set at a level suff:.cient to provide a minimum debt service
coverage of 1.5. A price of $155 per ton would be sufficient to satisfy this
criterion; it was suggested by th£e mission and accepted by the Government
as a basis for the financial analysis. This price is about 50% higher than
the  1974   level and is conservative, based on the expected prices that the
present factories may have to charge in early 1978. Increasing operating
costs for chemicals, spare parts, salaries and the scheduled overhaul of the
existing factories indicate that 1978 ex-factory prices may be more than $155
per metric ton. Nonetheless, the social objectives of the Government may
dictate keeping the price at about this level.
C. Cost Escalation Beyond 1978
5.         On the assumption that inflation rates for both the ex-factory
price and the production costs would be about the same, and the assurances
from the Government that the ex-factory prices will be set to meet a minimum
debt service coverage of 1.5, all costs and prices are kept at their mid-
1978 level throughout the twelve-year forecast period.
D. Financial Forecasts
6.         Annex 6-5 contains the 'projected financial statements.  A loss of
$3 million is anticipated for the initial five operating months because of
the assumed capacity utilization of 50%. This loss can be avoided if capacity
utilization reached 61%. This relatively high level of capacity utilization
is needed to breakeven during the period because part of the output, about
43,200 tons representing 8.2% of rated capacity, are used to build up the
finished goods inventory level. The initial operating loss is carried forward
to offset part of the following year 's taxable profit. Net profit is about
11% of gross revenue during the second year and 1.4% during the fourth year.
The percentage then improves by 0.8% during each subsequent year. The debt
service coverage ratio is 2.2 init-.ally and improves to 2.7 during the second
year.  It decrines to about 1.5 during the third year as full long-term debt
repayments coAmaence. But the ratio gradually improves to 1.6 by the fifth
year and 2.0 by the tenth year.
7.         The operating cash level. is determined by a minimum quick ratio of
1.2 or current ratio of 1.5 for ;ach of the projection years. However, a minimum
cash balance of $1.0 million is retained (see Annex 5-2) if the current or
quick ratio test indicates a smaller amount. In the balance sheets of Annex
6-5, no dividend payments or additional investments are assumed. Thus, retained
earnings are accumulated. Funds ir excess of the operating cash requirement
are isolated in the balance sheet as either funds available for distribution,
representing all of accumulated retained earnings, or as funds available for
investment, representing that portion of fixed assets converted into liquid
assets through a portion of the derreciation charges. These latter funds can
be used for fixed asset investments (e.g. expansion or renewal of equipment)
but cannot be paid out as dividends since they are not part of retained earnings.
The accumulation of these funds is fairly rapid during the intial four years,
starting at $2.5 million during the first year and reaching a cumulative total
of $2h.7 million during the fourth year.  There is an additional flow of about
$3.7 million during each subsequent year, up to the eleventh year.



AMNEX 6-7
Page 3
8.          The debt equity ratio is 58 :h3 after five months of operation
and gradually improves as shown in Annex 6-5. O the asswmptien that the
maximum dividend payments are made (i.e. all retained earnings are paid out),
then the debt/share capital ratio of Annex 6-5 should be used. This ratio
does not improve as fast as the debt equity ratio but is still very sound.
9.          Annex 6-6 provides several breakeven charts for the enterprise.
The capacity breakeven chart (Chart 1) gives the capacity utilization during
each year that is needed for either: (a) a profit breakeven or, (b) a cash
breakeven or, (c) a debt service coverage of 1.5, given an ex-factory price
of $155 per ton. The 1.5 debt service coverage curve in the chart gives the
capacity utilization that will provide a debt service coverage ratio of
exactly 1.5. Any capacity utilization above that curve will lead to a debt
service coverage ratio in excess of 1.5. A profit breakeven operation is
realized if sales revenues are exactly equal to full production costs (i.e.,
including depreciation and interest charges). Consequently, pre-tax profit
is zero. A cash breakeven operation is realized when the cash inflow from
sales revenue is exactly equal to the cash production cost (i.e., including
interest but excluding depreciation) plus the loan principal repayments.
At the cash-breakeven operation, the annual net cash inflow is zero. Any
capacity utilization below the profit breakeven curve will rsu.lt in an
operating loss. Similarly, any capacity utilization above the cash break-
even curve will generate a poaitive net cash inflow.
10.         Chart 2 of Annex 6-6 is the price breakeven chart.  It provides
the ex-factory price level that is necessary for a profit breakeven or a
cash breakeven or a 1.5 debt service coverage ratio operation, given the
capacity utilization for that particular year. The curve is interpreted in
the same manner as the capacity breakeven chart. For example, any price
level above the 1.5 debt service coverage price curve will result in a debt
service coverage ratio in excess of 1.5. That is, the gross cash generation
from sales will exceed the cash production costs and the loan principal re-
payments for that year by more than 50%. The cash breakeven price is
equivalent to a debt service coverage ratio of 1.0. That is, the gross cash
generation from sales will just equal the cash production costs and the loan
principal repayments.
11.         The profit breakeven price is less than $155 per metric ton,
except for the initial five month period. The 1.5 debt service coverage
price is about $155 per ton during the third year, but is less than $155 per
ton for all other years.
E. Financial Rate of Return and Sensitivity Analysis
12.         Capital costs are converted to mid-1978 constant dollars in
Annex 6-8 and the financial cost and benefit streams are shown in Annex 6-9.
Annex 6-10 contains the internal rates of return for the project under
different asmumptions. The rate of return is mildly sensitive to project
asumptions, with the exception of substantial cost overruns in the 20%
region or higher. The base rate of return (after taxes) assming an ex-



ANNEX 6-7
Page E 
factory price of $155 is 9.2%. The rate of return changes by about 1.1
percentage points for each $15 per ton change in the ex-factory price.
The before-tax rate of return is about 3-5 percentage points higher than
the after-tax level for an ex-factory price of $155 per ton. This
differential increases by about 0.7-1.0 percentage points for each $15
per ton increase in the price.
13.         The rate of return (after tax) is low relative to similar pro-
jects for the following reasons: (a) conservative assumptions about the
construction period, estimated at 52 months commencing from April 197h,
about  ten  months longer than other similar projects; (b) high capital
costs due to the price escalations in the capital equipment market, the
grassroots nature of the project and the import duties of ho%; (c) Con-
servative assumptiona about the ex-factory price; and (d) the high
corporate tax rate of 55%.
F. Financial Management and Product Pricing
1.         The import, product-Lon and distribution of fertilizers to the
district level are undertaken by Government enterprises. The pricing of
fertilizers at the various levels is also determined by the Government.
Since product pricing is the Ganoernment's function, while the financial
management of the project is thet responsibility of the enterprise, it is
important that the Government's pricing decisions are consistent with the
continued financial viability of' the project. It is therefore proposed
that the following financial guidelines be followed by the enterprise:
(a) the debt equity ratio should. not exceed 60:40; (b) the current ratio
and the debt service ratio should be at least 1.5:1 and dividends paid
only out of retained earnings subject to the above ratios; and (c) the
Government should set the ox-factory price at a level sufficient to meet
the debt service coverage ratio of 1.5:1.
15.         The financial guidelines, while indicating how prices should
be set in response to cost changes and liquidity considerations, does not
provide dircetions on how costs can be controlled and how high production
efficiency cin be achieved or su3tained. The productive bonus system
partly encournges high ^ipacity atilization, which is imperative.  The
methods for controlling coste and for obtaining high production efficiencies
and the design of an appropriate management system should be provided by the
expatriate management assistance team envisioned for this project.
16.         Costs can be properly controlled if they are monitored accurately
and if there are norms against wilch the factory operations can be compared.
The norms should preferably be batsed on efficient operating factories in a
competitive environment in both (leveloped and developing countries. To
monitor costs accurately, a cost accounting system will have to be instituted.
9uch a system must have sufficient detail and sufficiently short feedback
response to enable management to detect any adverse development as early as
possible. The expatriate managexient assistance in the area of financial
management would be expected to ostablish some system along these lines.
