BANGIADESH - AS;,UGANJ FERTILIZER PROJECT
ECoIiOMIC ANALYSIS
A. Basis for Economic Analysis
1.        The economic analysis is based on 1978 economic operating costs as
shown in Annex 6-3, and a 1978 economic price of US$175 per ton of bagged
urea fertilizer.  This is roughly composed of an equivalent f.o.b. (Thdonezia
or Persian Gulf) price of $145 ler ton (bagged), plus a freight charge of
$25 per ton and $5 per ton for local port handling. This price is consistent
with the recent Bank forecasts. No adjustments for inland freight are made
since distribution costs from t1Le port of entry and from the plant site would
most likely be of the same order of magnitude. The economic wage rates are
assumed to be the same as the wage rates used in the financial analysis.
Since most of the labor employed in the project are skilled or management
personnel, end since the labor bill is not a large proportion of the total
economic production costs, this assumption is acceptable. Import duties and
the interest during construction are excluded from the economic capital
costs. The official foreign exchange rate of Tk 7.50 per US dollar is used
to convert local costs to foreign exchange units. Capital costs are con-
verted to mid-1978 dollars in Anaex 6-8.
B. The Shadow Price of Natural las
2.        There is no generally accepted method of estimating the shadow price
of exhaustible resources.  Conseluently, the more commonly used approach of
distinguishing between tradeable as opposed to non-tradeable commodities as
a basis for deriving shadow prices will be used.
3.        This report assumes thLt the natural gas is a non-tradeable
commodity. This judgement is based primarily on three factors: (a) The
present proven reserves of the inldividual gasfields are not large enough to
justify a liquified natural gas (tNG) facility; (b) Pooling of these fields
by a pipeline system is technically difficult or too expensive because of the
geography, river systems and soil characteristics; and (c) Bangladesh will
not import any additional crude oil or fuels as a result of the proposed
project since almost all economically and technically feasible substitution
of gas for fuel oil has been made.
4.        As of March 1974, the ratural gas price had the following component
per MSCF:
a. Well-head (producer's) price       Tk 0.50
b. Transmission charges               Tk 0.70
c.  Excise duty                       Tk 0.40
Total                        TkS1.60
5.        The Titas Gas Transemission and Distribution Company (TGTDC, a
public corporation), proposed to raise the transmission charges to Tk 1.42
per MSCF by mid-1974. This was sabsequently approved by the Government.



ANNEX 7-1
Page 2
For purposes of this project, however, a delivered price of Tk 3.72 per
MSCF (60% higher than the new price) was set by the Government and incor-
porated in the gas contract agreement between AFCC and TGTDC. No breakdown
of the excise duty component was projected. Since the Government sets the
gas price, owns the transmission company, and is part owner of the gasfields
operating company, it is difficult to estimate the market pricejand even as-
suming the gas is not tradeable, an economic price for that matter. Hence,
it will be assumed that the economic price is identical to the price used
in the financial analysis of Tk 3.72 (US$0.50) per MSCF.
6.        If the opposite assumption  that the gas is a tradeable commodity
is taken, then the gas might be priced at an energy basis of US$1.40 per
million BTU, which is equivalent to about US$9-10 per barrel of crude oil.
At US$1 .40 per million BTU, the Titas gas will be priced at about US$1.31
per MSCF as the average lower heating value of the gas is 938 BTU per SCF.

COMPARATIVE COST ADVANTAGE AND EFICIENCY OF IMPORT SUBSTITUTION
A. Introduction
1.        For projects with tradeable outputs (i.e., import substitutes
or export products), the concept of comparative advantage is generally
used for comparing the production efficiencies of different countries
with respect to these tradeable outputs. The theory of comparative
advantage is still evolving and several methodologies have been proposed.
Comparative advantage analysis is essentially derived from or related to,
the net present value (NPV) approach to economic analysis. Thus, it is
an alternative way of analyzing the economic desirability of projects, in
the same way that the net present value approach is an alternative, but
closely allied approach to the internal economic rate of return (IER)
method. However, the comparative advantage measures provide a better indi-
cator for inter-country comparisons as opposed to the global type of result
that the IER method yields. As an example, assume that urea fertilizer
projects (identical capacities) in Country A and Country B both have an
18% IER. This fact alone is not sufficient to form a judgement as to which
country has a more efficient project or comparative advantage. Should the
opportunity cost of capital be different, say 10% for Coumtry A and 15% for
Country B, then Country A would have a comparative advantage over Country
B. Thus, for projects whose outputs are in active international trade, and
certainly for export oriented proAects, some measure of comparative advan-
tage is desirable in addition to the IER measure. This is especially true
if comparisons of projects in different countries are necessary. Such
would be the case if the implementation of most of the planned p2rojects
could create a potential for large international surpluses (or undesirably
low world prices), threatening the foreign exchange earnings of some develop-
ing (producer) countries or reducing the economic viability of these import
substituting projects unless the number of projects are limited.
B. Domestic Resource Costs of Foreign Exchange Earned or Saved
2.        Following the methodology suggested by Brunol/, comparative advan-
tage will be measured in terms of the domestic resource costs (flRC) incurred
per unit of foreign exchange earned or saved. Comparative advantage is then
the efficiency with which the project transforms domestic resources into
foreign exchange. Within a multi-period framework, DRC is the ratio of the
discounted net present value of domeatic economic costs and benefits
(expressed in domestic currency), to the net preaent value of the economic
1/ Bruno, M., "The Optimal Selection of Eixport-Promoting and Import-
Substituting Projects.-"  In Planning the External Sector: Techniques,
Problems and Policies. New Yte  Nations 1 967.And "Domestic
Resource Costs and Effective Protection: Clarification and Synthesis."
J.P.E. 80 (Jan/Feb. 1972: 16-33).



ANNEX 7-5
Page 2
foreign exchange coats and benlefits (expressed in foreign exchange),
uwing the opportunity cost of capital as the discount factor. Thus,
all the information needed to calculate the DRC of a project is contained
in the information needed by &rn NPV calculation.  The cost and benefit
streams in an NPV calculation are simply separated into a domestic cost
or benefit component and a foreign exchange cost or benefit component.
3.        Suppose:

6.        In general, the NPV criterion for project selection is to accept
the project if the NPV is greater than or equal to zero and to reject the
project otherwise. This is identical to accepting the project if the
1RC is less than or equal to the shadow exchange rate and rejecting the
project otherwise.
7.        The cost and benefit streams in foreign exchange and domestic
currency for the DRC computation are shown in Table 1.
C. Efficiency of Import Substitution
8.        For import substituting projects, the ratio of the DRC to the
shadow exchange rate I will be defined as the efficiency of import
substitution (EIS). The ratio of DRC to B is an absolute unitless ratio
and is interpreted as the units of domestic resources expressed in foreign
exchange required to save one unit of foreign exchange. For example, a
ratio of 0.6 iwplies US$60 equivalent of domestic resources are used by a
project to save (in the form of imports) US$100 in foreign exchange.
9.        The efficiency of import substitution can be used directly to
evaluate projects in the same way that the NPV or the DRC measures are
used. Furthermore, since the BIS incorporaSes both the opportunity cost
of capital and the shadow exchange rate, it can be used for inter-country
comparisons. In project appraisal, the different measures are used as
follows:

1.        For Bangladesh, the opportunity cost of capital is difficult to
estimate. Intuitively, one can ask what would be the minimum internal
economic rate of return acceptable to IDA for porjects in the country.
Considering the IER of past IDA financed projects in Bangladesh or similar
countries, 15% appears to be a reasonable cut-off point implying a range
of 10 to 15% for the opportunity cost of capital.  If 15% is used as a
rough estimate of the opportunity cost of capital, then the efficiency of
import substitution (EIS) would be 0.48 (say 0.5), at the official exchange
rate and 0.26 at the shadow exchange rate of Tk 1 per US dollar.  Within
the range of 10 to 15% for the opportunity cost of capital, the EIS would
range from 0.3 to 0.5 based on the official exchange rate. Thus, the
project represents a highly efficient scheme for transforming domestic
resources into foreign exchange savings.
a/ When the opportunity cost of capital is set equal to the internal rate of
return (IER), then NPV is zero, DRC is equal to the shadow exchange rate
and EIS is equal to one.
